-110-                       ANNEX6
ANNEX 6: LAND ACQUISITION AND RESETTLEMENT
ACTION PLAN
A. INTRODUCTION
1.    The implementation of the Yunnan Environment Project (YEP) entails land
acquisition and resettlement/rehabilitation of affected persons.  In order to facilitate
mitigation of the adverse land acquisition related impacts of the project, YEPO has prepared
a Resettlement Action Plan.
2.    The Resettlement Action Plan (RAP) for YEP covers project components located in
the cities of Kunming, Qujing and Gejiu; and in several sites in the Dianchi Basin. This
annex describes the likely land acquisition and resettlement impacts of YEP and discusses
the framework developed by the Provincial and Municipal Governnents for implementing
and financing land acquisition and resettlement activities. Section B describes the impacts
of the project and the surveys carried out to measure them. Section C sets out the
implementation framework for Land Acquisition and Resettlement (LAR).  YEP
implementation would commence in 1996 and continue to 2001.
B. PROJECT IMPACTS
3.    The following project components would require land acquisition and/or
resettlement/rehabilitation of affected persons:
Provincewide
(a)   Environmental Management and Monitoring Project;
Kunming City
(b)   Sewage Treatment Works Number 1;
(c)   Sewage Treatment Works Number 4;
(d)   Sewage Treatment Works Number 5 (East);
(e)   West Suburban Sewer Networks;
(f)    Municipal Sewer Networks;
(g)   Municipal Water Supply;
(h)   Municipal Solid Waste;
(i)   Industrial Pollution Control;



- 111 -                     ANNEX6
Dianchi Basin
(j)    Chenggong Sewage Treatment;
(k)   Jinning Sewage Treatment;
(1)    Rural Sanitation;
Qujing
(m)   Urban Sewage Treatment;
(n)   Urban Solid Waste;
(o)   Urban Water Supply; and
Gejiu
(p)   Urban Sewage Treatment.
4.    Construction of these project components requires land acquisition, together with
the rehabilitation and/or resettlement of the affected persons. In order to determine the
impact of project components on the affected people and property each implementing
agency decided to conduct a census survey of affected persons and properties and to collect
socioeconomic data.
Census Survey
5.    A Census Survey has been carried out in stages commencing in July 1995 in
collaboration with the local governments. The detailed surveys are progressively updated
as the technical designs are finalized. The data set out here represent the situation by
March 29, 1996. Most of the project components have been approved to the Feasibility
Study Stage. The Feasibility Study usually sets the broad parameters for land acquisition,
following which the implementing agencies can notify the affected townships, and the
detailed census of affected persons and properties can begin. There would be small changes
as the Design Studies for project components are completed and approved. Since some
census surveys have still to be finalized in sites for which boundaries have been recently
changed, some figures presented here may be subject to minor change.
6.    Preliminary impacts are summarized as follows:
(a)   The total amount of land to be acquired is 215 hectares. Most of the sites
are greenfield sites on the urban fringes;
(b)   About 100 hectares of land would be borrowed temporarily during
construction;
(c)   No houses need to be relocated;
(d)   A total of 3,090 persons are affected by land acquisition;



-112 -                      ANNEX6
(e)   Most of the affected persons can be reabsorbed back into agriculture through
land redistribution, but 890 people (including dependents) would need to be
provided with nonagricultural, enterprise jobs. The latter group will be
offered a choice between jobs in enterprises, or cash compensation in lieu of
jobs. All workers, regardless of age, will be offered cash compensation at
the rate needed to maintain incomes;
(f)   The project will affect about 70 workers in 4 enterprises until enterprise
production facilities are reconstructed.
7.    Preliminary socioeconomic data regarding the affected persons are being collected
by the implementing agencies. Before each contract is initiated an appointed institution
would conduct detailed baseline socioeconomic surveys of the affected people in each
contract.  This work would be completed before initiation of land acquisition and
resettlement activities for each project component. The baseline survey would measure the
income levels and standards of living of the affected persons before land acquisition and
resettlement. This baseline survey would form the basis for comparing their income levels
and standards of living as reported by the monitoring reports during project implementation.
Minimization of Land Acquisition and Resettlement
8.    Most of the Feasibility Studies included an analysis of land acquisition and possible
resettlement, as part of the identification of project options. While the selection between
options was based on technical and economic criteria, the potential impact on project
affected persons was also considered. For example, the Kunming Water Supply and Solid
Waste projects have endeavored to select sites further from concentrated populations that
minimize disruption to communities. The Maliohe Reservoir Xiyuan Tunnel Stage 2
project component would have inundated three villages and relocated some 1,500 people.
The YEP has excluded this project component on technical, economical and social grounds.
After careful review the number of people requiring relocation of housing has been reduced
to zero.
C. FRAMEWORK FOR IMPLEMENTATION OF LAND ACQUISITION AND
RESETTLEMENT ACTIVITIES
9.    The RAP describes the framework for implementing the land acquisition and
resettlement program. The framework comprises the following elements:
(a)   Objectives and principles of land compensation and resettlement;
(b)   Legal framework and resettlement policies;
(c)   Provisions for job creation
(d)   Resettlement Institutions and Agencies



-113 -                      ANNEX6
(e)   Costs and budget
(f)   Implementation schedule and arrangements for resettlement
(g)   Participation and consultation with affected persons
(h)   Redressal of grievance and appeals process
(i)    Monitoring and evaluation of RAP implementation.
10.   The RAP, covering the above components of resettlement implementation
framework, has been prepared by YEPO based on discussions with the concerned levels of
government; the resulting RAP has been approved by the concerned provincial and
municipal authorities, and would be implemented as an integral part of the YEP. The
components of the frarnework for implementation are described below.
Objectives and Principles of Resettlement
11.   The main objective of the land acquisition and resettlement (LAR) is to ensure that
the standards of living and income levels of all categories of affected persons are improved,
or at least restored, after resettlement. The main principles for implementing the LAR
program are as follows:
(a)   Minimize land acquisition and resettlement as much as possible through
choice of project components and changes in design and alignment;
(b)   Pay compensation for all structures at the replacement value of those
structures;
(c)   Pay compensation for collective land to the local collective.  This
compensation would be used to increase the productive capacity of the
collectives and would not be used for any other purpose.
(d)   Agencies will arrange jobs in nonagricultural enterprises for persons whose
lands are acquired and who are left with less than the economic per capita
landholding, to ensure that their income levels and standard of living are at
least restored, or improved.
(e)   All the civic facilities, such as municipal roads, communication, lighting,
sewerage, treatment work, etc. will be restored at the new locations. The
YEP will fully compensate for transfer and reconstruction at replacement
cost.
(f)    The YEP will reconstruct all affected enterprises at nearby locations at the
same standard as before.



-114-                       ANNEX6
(g)   The YEP will compensate and resettle persons affected by temporary land
borrow to restore their standards of living.
(h)   YEP agencies will plan and implement land acquisition and resettlement
activities with the participation of affected persons and their representatives.
(i)    The affected persons or enterprises have avenues for redress of their
grievances and if they are not satisfied with the outcome, they can appeal to
higher authority and to civil courts.
12.   The situation of persons whose lands are affected has been reviewed by YEPO and
the implementing agencies.  They would be offered a choice between jobs in
nonagricultural enterprises and self-employment with a cash compensation payment. The
cost ranges from Y 13,000 to Y 15,000 in Kunming and is Y 10,000 in Qujing and Gejiu
and will be finalized taking into account the labor market situation and income cost from
farming. Those opting for jobs would be paid a transition allowance until jobs are provided.
In addition to income restoration for older people at the same rates, special rehabilitation
measures will be taken to ensure that elderly affected persons are able to maintain their
standards of living. The YEP implementing agencies will raise these rates if they are not
sufficient to maintain standards of living.
13.   The following guidelines and principles would be followed:
(a)   New houses of the affected persons would be of at least the same standard as
the affected houses or equal to the minimum current standard, whichever is
the greater;
(b)   All civic infrastructure would be replaced at project cost;
(c)   All affected enterprises would be assisted to resume operations at nearby
locations provided by the local governments;
(d)   Workers would be provided with allowances equivalent to their salaries for
the period of closure of their enterprises; and
(e)   Planning and implementation of the LAR program would be carried out with
the participation of the affected persons and their representatives
14.   The affected persons and enterprises would be able to redress their grievances, if
any, from the LAR program, and if they are not satisfied, can appeal to higher authorities
and courts.
Legal Framework and Resettlement Policies
15.   The RAP has been prepared based on the following laws, regulations and guidelines
for land acquisition and resettlement issued by various authorities in China:



-115 -                      ANNEX6
(a)   The People's Republic of China Land Management Law;
(b)   The People's Republic of China Land Management Implementation Act;
(c)   Rules Of Land Acquisition For National Construction;
(d)   Land Administration Implementation Measures of Yunnan Province 1994;
(e)   Regulations To Handle Transfer Of Nonagricultural Land In Acquiring
Collective Farmland For National Construction in Yunnan Province;
(f)    Measures To Arrange Surplus Labor After Land Acquisition For National
Construction issued by Kumning Municipal Government in 1989:
(i)    Notice on compensation for vegetable land issued by Kunming
Municipal Government;
(ii)    Yunnan Province: Implementation of Measures of People's Republic
of China Temporary rules of Land Utilization Tax;
(iii)    Yunnan Provincial Government: Implementing Measures of Land
Acquisition Tax;
(iv)    Urgent Notice on Implementing Land Administrative Laws And
Regulations issued by Qujing Municipal Government; and
(v)    Specific Measures Of Land Administration In Honghe Hani-Yi
Autonomous Prefecture (Draft) issued by Honghe Prefecture
Government in 1990.
16.   The implementation of land acquisition and resettlement program would be carried
out in accordance with the specific provisions of the Resettlement Action Plan (RAP).
Compensation/Resettlement Policies for Different Categories of Project Impacts
17.   The following categories of project impacts we assessed during the census survey of
affected persons and properties:
(a)   Permanent land acquisition of collective land;
(b)   Temporary acquisition of collective land;
(c)   Loss of housing;
(d)   Partial impacts on houses and other structures;
(e)   Relocation of various types of enterprises; state, TVE and private;



-116-                      ANNEX6
(f)   Temporarily affected persons;
(g)   Loss of employment in agriculture;
(h)   Temporary loss of employment in agriculture due to temporary land
acquisition;
(i)   Impacts on public infrastructure and other assets
0)    Loss of standing crops;
(k)   Indirect impacts in the disturbance zone; and
(1)    Relocation allowance.
18.   The compensation and/ or resettlement policies for the above categories of impacts
are as follows:
(a)   Permanent Land Acquisition Of Collective Land. The borrower or
implementing agencies would pay compensation to the affected township
government. The rates have been provided in the RAP. The township
governments would use compensation payments to develop the productive
potential of the township residents through the development of agriculture-
based and nonagriculture-based economic activities;
(b)   Temporary Leasing of Land During Construction. Borrower agencies
would compensate for temporary land leasing at the rate of up to
Y 10,000 mu 'for a period of half to one year, based on gross agricultural
output value of the land.  With this compensation local township
governments would arrange the economic rehabilitation of persons affected
temporarily;
(c)   Loss of Housing. Persons losing government owned or private apartments
and houses in Kunming, Qujing and Gejiu would have the options of:
(i)    moving into a community-built house of the same standard as
previous house, without any extra payment; and
(ii)   self-constructing a house upon payment of compensation at
replacement value for the affected house or structure. No deductions
would be made for depreciation.
I mu = 0.0667 ha or 0.1648 ac.



-117 -                      ANNEX6
19.   The RAP provides full details of housing allocation and reconstruction options, as
follows:
(a)   Partial Impacts on Structures.  Borrower agencies would pay full
replacement value as compensation;
(b)   Impact on Enterprises. Land Departments would, in consultation with
local governments, allocate alternative plots to affected enterprises.
Borrower agencies would compensate enterprises at replacement rates for
affected buildings, structures and other fixtures and furnishings. They
would pay the full cost of transfer of facilities. They would compensate
affected workers in lieu of salaries at rates equivalent to the monthly salaries
of the workers.  They would compensate affected TVE and private
enterprises for net losses in production during the period when production is
stopped;
(c)   Shops and Small Businesses. Land Departments, in consultation with local
governments, would provide such businesses with alternative plots for
continuing their businesses. Borrower agencies would compensate such
businesses for the net losses in income during the period when their business
is closed;
(d)   Temporarily Affected Enterprises. Borrower agencies would compensate
such enterprises at replacement value with regard to special features of
construction. No deduction would be made for depreciation. Arrangements
would be made to provide temporary alternative sites to affected enterprises.
Staff would be compensated for loss of salary and production losses would
be compensated;
(e)   Loss Of Employment In Agriculture.  All farmers forced out of
agriculture by land acquisition would be given the option of self-
employment or jobs in  nonagricultural enterprises.   The project
implementing agencies would pay an amount of Y 13,000-15,000/ person to
the enterprises for providing jobs to affected persons. During the relocation
period they would pay workers Y 150/month as a subsistence allowance. In
case the affected persons do not receive jobs within three months from the
date of land acquisition, the borrower agencies would provide temporary
jobs to them until they can be provided jobs in enterprises. In case the
affected persons want to opt for self-employment, they would be provided
with the same amount in lieu of jobs in enterprises. Affected persons above
age of 50 years would be eligible for income restoration at the same rate. In
addition to this, specific measures would be taken by the borrower agencies
and the local governments to ensure that the standards of living of the
elderly persons are restored after relocation;



-118 -                      ANNEX6
(f)   Temporary Loss Of Employment In Agriculture.  The borrower
implementing agencies would pay the respective township governments up
to Y 10,000 per mu of land affected by temporary land borrow. With the
help of this compensation amount, the local governments would plan
rehabilitation activities for affected persons.   Upon completion of
construction, the borrower agencies would return the land to the local
township after restoring it to its original condition;
(g)   Impact On Public Infrastructure And Other Assets.  All civic
infrastructure would be compensated at replacement rates without any
deduction for depreciation. The infrastructure would be reconstructed by the
respective departments;
(h)   Loss Of Standing Crops. Standing crops compensation would be paid
directly to the affected farmers at the full market value of affected crops;
(i)   Indirect Impacts In The Disturbance Zone. Indirect impacts, if any, due
to the construction of the project, on structures in the disturbance zone
would be compensated for at the full replacement rates. Structures needing
repairs would be compensated fully for the repairs. If any houses need to be
relocated due to such impact, they would be treated on the same lines as
houses affected by permanent land acquisition; and
(j)    Relocating households would be paid at full replacement values.
20.  In case it were found that the standards of living of any category of affected persons
have not been restored after relocation or resettlement, the borrower implementing agency
would be responsible to take additional measures to ensure that the main objective of the
RAP is to be achieved.
Job Creation
21.   All of the persons forced out of agriculture by land acquisition would be provided
with jobs in nonagricultural enterprises or have the option of self-employment. The number
of persons to be provided jobs is calculated on the basis of maintaining the average per
capita land holding in Yunnan Province and other involved cities. Out of the 3,090 affected
persons, some 890 are eligible for new employment. This includes those older workers who
will be offered income restoration at the cash compensation rate for self-employment. Jobs
to the eligible persons would be provided through one of the following methods:
(a)   Provide jobs in state enterprises;
(b)   Provide jobs in collective (township and village) enterprises;
(c)   Private enterprises; and



-119 -                     ANNEX6
(d)   Self-employment
22.   Job creation is the responsibility of the borrower implementing agencies assisted by
the Yunnan Provincial and other Cities Land Management Bureau, and the Labor Bureau at
various levels. Arrangements to provide jobs to eligible labor are necessary before the
Construction Land Use Certificate is issued by the planning and land administrative
departments in each project city. The borrower implementing agencies would pay a sum to
be determined per worker to township enterprises and a sum to be determined per worker to
other enterprises for job creation. In case the affected person opts for self-employment
he/she would be paid a resettlement subsidy of a minimum of sum to be determined and an
additional sum to be determined for each additional year.
23.   Jobs should be provided within one month of signing the agreement for land
acquisition and resettlement between borrowers and the Local Land Acquisition Bureau.
However, if they jobs are not provided within three months of land acquisition, borrower
agencies would provide temporary jobs to affected persons until enterprise jobs can be
provided. The wage level for the temporary jobs would be at least equivalent to the
minimum wage level in the municipality/county. They would pay a monthly subsistence
allowance to each eligible worker for a period of three months. The names of enterprises
that would provide jobs are set out in the RAP.
24.   The persons eligible for job creation would be trained in specific skills before they
are employed on the job. The training would be provided by agencies to be determined.
Out of the resettlement subsidy to be paid to enterprises Y 200/worker would be reserved
especially for training.
Resettlement Institutions and Agencies
25.   The Yunnan Provincial Government (YPG) has vested overall responsibility for
coordination of planning and implementation of resettlement activities with YEPO. YEPO
and the Municipal project offices in Kunming, Qujing and Gejiu are responsible for
coordinating resettlement activities.  The actual planning and implementation of
resettlement would be carried out by the borrower implementing agencies together with
local land administration departments, township and village governments. Apart from
these, the main departments, bureaus and agencies responsible for various aspects of LAR
planning, implementing, coordinating and monitoring resettlement activities are the
following:
(a)   The Planning Administration Office in each project city;
(b)   The Yunnan Provincial and Municipal Land Administration Bureau for
Kunming, Qujing and Gejiu;
(c)   The Labor Bureau for Kunming, Qujing and Gejiu; and



-120 -                     ANNEX6
(d)   Research Institute of Foreign Capital Introduction and Utilization,
Southwest Communication University (Monitoring and Evaluation Agency).
26.  In addition to the above, YEPO would convene a Land Acquisition and
Resettlement (LAR) Group with members from all involved agencies. This group would
meet every four months throughout resettlement implementation to resolve coordination
issues.
27.   Staff of YEPO have attended the Resettlement Training Program organized by the
World Bank at Nanjing in April 1995. The staff who received training at Nanjing have
been conducting inhouse training programs in YEPO and other project offices. Officials of
key departments of YPG also attended the regular discussions and seminars with YEPO
officials. YEPO would organize a training program at times to be determined throughout
resettlement implementation.
Costs and Budget
28.   The costs of RAP implementation would be a part of the overall budget of the YEP.
The annual requirement of funds would be included in the annual investment plan for the
project. The base cost of land acquisition and resettlement is estimated to be approximately
Y 156 million. The detailed cost estimates (shown annually) would be provided. Each
borrower implementing agency would pay the funds for RAP implementation to the
respective labor and land administration departments, state and collective enterprises and
public utility companies. They would spend the funds based on the specific provisions of
the RAP.
29.   Borrower agencies would pay 4 percent of the total LAR cost to cover the
administrative expenses to the Land Administration Departments responsible for
implementing LAR agreements. The cost estimates would be adjusted annually based on
the actual inflation rate for the past year. Physical contingencies of 15 percent of the cost
estimates would also be budgeted. Each borrower implementing agency is responsible for
provision of adequate funds to carry out RAP implementation. Cost overruns, if any, would
be incurred by each such agency.
Implementation of Land Acquisition and Resettlement
30.   There would be 16 separate contracts with separate "startup" dates of construction
ranging from July 1996. The land acquisition and resettlement activities would be specified
in detail in the RAP. On any given contract RAP activities should be completed at least one
month before the proposed date of start of construction on that contract.  Each
implementing agency would certify before the start of any given contract that the LAR
activities on that contract have been completed. Annex 8 sets out the proposed startup and
completion dates for the various contracts. A "Generic Implementation Schedule" has been
prepared based on the estimated time likely to be taken for each activity under the LAR
program. This can be used to arrive at the specific dates of completion of the various land



-121-                        ANNEX6
acquisition and resettlement activities in any contract by providing the proposed startup date
for resettlement activities.
Participation and Consultations
31.   The LAR program has been designed with the active involvement of affected
persons and their representatives. The affected persons are informed about the project after
the Feasibility Study is approved by the Municipal and Provincial Planning Commissions.
The affected persons and their representatives participated in the census surveys carried out
by each implementing agency and project office. A notice of LAR would be given to each
affected household after the project is approved by the Bank/IDA. The borrower agencies,
assisted by local government, would inform the affected persons and households about the
project, the proposed resettlement and the specific entitlements of each affected household.
Each borrower agency would also locate a field office, easily accessible to the affected
persons, within the affected area, in order to provide information to affected persons and
resolve their grievances. The affected persons would participate in the following activities:
(a)    Select specific resettlement locations for residential sites and for enterprises;
(b)   Construct their own houses (depending on their option);
(c)    Transfer their assets to the new houses;
(d)    Select enterprises for jobs;
(e)    Exercise the option of self-employment; and
(f)    Manage implementation of the LAR  program  through their village,
township representatives.
Resettlement Information Booklet
32.   Resettlement Information Booklets are being prepared for each affected site. The
booklets will have a common section with overall regulations and principles and a section
laying out the resettlement arrangements specific to each affected area. The wide variation
among cities leads to different solutions in several cases. The booklet will be given to each
affected person, ensuring their knowledge of their rights under the project, and avenues of
appeal of their rights, are respected.
Redressal of Grievances and the Appeals Process
33.   In order to ensure that the affected persons have avenues for redressing their
grievances, detailed procedures for redressal of grievances would be established. This
procedure is likely to comprise certain stages. First, the affected person could lodge a
grievance with the local township Land Management Bureau, to which the Bureau should
respond within 15 days. Second, in case the person is not satisfied, an appeal could be
made to a department within the District Government within a specified time frame from



-122 -                      ANNEX6
the date of response from the Land Management Bureau at township level. Third, if still
unsatisfied, the affected person could appeal to a department within the Municipal
Government for redressing the grievance. Finally, if still unsatisfied, an appeal can be
lodged with the People's Court within 15 days of receiving the decision from Municipal
level. For grievances related to job provision, the respective Labor Bureau at the various
levels would be the appropriate authorities. The grievance could be for any aspect of the
RAP program, including rates of compensation. Grievance mechanisms would be widely
publicized among the affected persons.
Monitoring and Evaluation
34.   In order to ensure that the implementation of the LAR program would be carried out
in accordance with the provisions of the RAP, detailed monitoring and evaluation, both
intemal and external, would be carried out, both during and after implementation of the
project.
(a)   Internal Monitoring. Each borrower agency would be responsible for
internal monitoring of the LAR program. This would be coordinated by
YEPO. Monitoring would be carried out based on the provisions of the
RAP. The main indicators to be monitored are payment of compensation,
allotment of housing in accordance with the options exercised, provision of
jobs or alternative agricultural land, restoration of public facilities, payment
of self-employment subsidy and restoration of production activities of
enterprises. The internal monitoring reports would be prepared every six
months. Preliminary monitoring formats have been prepared and would be
provided as an annex to the RAP. Internal monitoring would be carried out
on a quarterly basis and an internal monitoring report would be submitted to
the Bank/IDA every six months. The first internal monitoring report will be
submitted on December 31, 1996. The report would be discussed in the next
meeting of LAR Group which would also recommend detailed follow-up
measures.
(b)   External Monitoring. An independent agency would be contracted to
conduct extemal monitoring and evaluation of the LAR program. In
addition to verifying the results of the internal monitoring reports, this
institute would monitor the following qualitative indicators: (i) timeliness
and adequacy of compensation payments; (ii) the linkage of land acquisition
and resettlement; (iii) timely provision of appropriate jobs or altemative
agricultural land; (iv) adequate restoration of civic infrastructure;
(v) relocation of enterprises in accordance with the policy provisions
contained in the RAP; (vi) provision of altemative housing in accordance
with the option exercised and in a timely manner; (vii) levels of satisfaction
among the affected persons; (viii) standards of living after relocation. In
order to measure the standards of living after resettlement, this institute



-123 -                     ANNEX6
would conduct another socioeconomic survey of the resettled persons about
one year after project completion.
35.   The methodology for carrying out extemal monitoring and evaluation has been
described in the RAP. The selected extemal monitoring institute would maintain a data
base with files on each affected household, which would be updated based on information
collected in successive rounds of monitoring. The Monitoring and Evaluation Report
would be prepared by this institute every six months, until construction of all subprojects is
completed. The first external monitoring report will be submitted to the Bank/IDA on
February 28, 1997. The report would be discussed in the next meeting of LAR group and
specific follow-up measures would be initiated by the LAR group. The report would
review the adequacy of implementation measures and report on the perceptions of the
affected persons. It would also identify critical issues in implementation and make
recommendations to improve overall effectiveness of the resettlement program. The report
would also report progress of follow-up in initiated by YEPO/LAR group and the borrower
implementing agencies on the recommendations of the previous monitoring reports.



-124 -                      ANNEX7
ANNEX 7: ENVIRONMENTAL ASSESSMENT SUMMARY
Introduction
1.    Yunnan Province is located in the southwest of China and is bordered to the north
by Sichuan Province, to the east by Guizhou and Guangxi Provinces and to the south and
west by Viet Nam, Lao PDR and Myanmar. The province has a total land area of
394,000 km2 and a population of 38 million, which is largely rural, some 81 percent being
involved in agriculture. The provincial capital and major industrial center, Kunming, is
by far the largest city (population 1.2 million) and dominates the economy of the
province. The next largest cities, also significant industrial centers, are Qujing and Gejiu,
with populations of 161,500 and 134,000, respectively.
2.    The northwest of the province is dominated by the southeastern Himalayas and
includes the sources of three major rivers, the Jingshajiang (which discharges to the
Changjiang (Yangtze), the Lancangjiang (Mekong) and the Nujiang (Salween). The
northeast area includes the source of the Nanpanjiang (Pearl River). The south of the
province is tropical and the central and eastern areas comprise a plateau and fertile lake
basins at an elevation of about 2,000 m.
3.    Rapid economic growth over the past 15 years has resulted in severe
environmental degradation in and around urban centers and even in some rural areas. The
environment in many areas in the province is under severe stress. The principal cause of
degradation of the water environment in urban areas is the lack of effective control of
polluting discharges to watercourses. The situation is exacerbated by the substantial
underpricing of water and environmental services such as wastewater disposal, which
leads to wasteful consumption and hence pressure on water resources.
4.    This is particularly the case in the Dianchi Lake basin, which includes Kunming,
where water resources are particularly limited and are threatened in terns both of water
quality and quantity. The Dianchi Lake receives discharges of municipal and industrial
wastewater from Kunming and its suburbs. However, the water resources of the Dianchi
Lake basin are very limited, per capita runoff being of the order of 5 percent of the
Yunnan and 3 percent of the world averages. As a consequence of the inadequate water
resources for Kunming, the lake provides up to 50 percent of the City's water supplies
even in an average year. Further increases in demand will result in even more reliance on
the lake for supplies of potable water.
5.    The repeated recycling of wastewater, consequent upon the use of the lake as a
major contribution to the Kunming water supply, has resulted in substantial increases in
the loads of nutrients, principally nitrogen and phosphorus, discharged to the lake. The
situation is exacerbated by the presence of a number of nitrogen and phosphorus-based



-125 -                      ANNEX7
fertilizer plants in the basin, exploiting the local phosphate-based mineral resources. The
lake receives discharges of nutrient-rich wastewater from these plants and also runoff
from agricultural areas in its catchment. As a result of the high nutrient loads, the
Dianchi Lake is already in an advanced state of eutrophication, having reached a
hypertrophic state. This has caused serious deterioration in water quality, which is
manifested in part in major problems in water treatment in Kunming, resulting in intake
closure and the introduction of more extensive water treatment processes.
6.    Growth in the other urban centers of Yunnan, principally Qujing and Gejiu,
coupled with inadequate control of polluting discharges, is causing similar problems of
water quality and pressure on limited water resources. A major environmental master
plan study for the Dianchi Basin, Qujing and Gejiu, funded by the UK Overseas
Development Administration (ODA) was completed in 1996. This study has identified a
set of major priority investments aimed at rehabilitation of water quality, principally in
Dianchi Lake, and improving the effectiveness of water resource utilization and wastes
management.
7.    In order to address the above concerns the proposed project would focus on
improving the state of the environment, principally in terms of water quality, via a range
of investments with the objectives of:
(a)   improving water quality, mainly in Dianchi Lake, and water resources
management to allow sustained safe beneficial use for public water supply;
(b)   improving standards of municipal wastes management;
(c)   institution of measures to strengthen control of industrial pollution and to
improve water and materials utilization;
(d)   enhancing facilities for the effective monitoring of environmental quality
in Yunnan; and
(e)   strengthening of policies and institutional arrangements for water quality
management, water and wastewater facilities operations, environmental
regulation and management training.
8.    Technical support and training would be provided to utility and environment
sector agencies in Yunnan at provincial and municipal levels, including environmental
protection bureaus (EPBs) and monitoring centers, municipal utility operations and
finance departments.
Administrative and Institutional Framework
9.    The State Environmental Protection Committee (SEPC) is the highest level of
authority on environmental policy and includes the State Council and heads of all
relevant ministries and agencies. The national Environmental Protection Agency (NEPA)



-126 -                     ANNEX7
functions as the secretariat of SEPC and is responsible for all aspects of environmental
policy although shares authority with other agencies for certain natural resources. NEPA
sets overall policies and regulations governing the provincial and municipal EPBs and
implementation of environmental policy is through the municipal and provincial EPBs,
who are responsible for the maintenance of enviromnental quality.
10.   Specific responsibility for water resources in Yunnan lies with the Yunnan Water
Conservancy Bureau, and with the Kunming Water Conservancy Bureau for the resources
of the Dianchi Lake basin. Maintenance of water quality is the responsibility of the
Yunnan EPB and that in the Dianchi Lake basin of the Kunming and other municipal
EPBs. These bureaus monitor environmental quality, set environmental and discharge
standards, assess and levy pollution charges and prosecute serious polluters.
Project Description
11.   The proposed project comprises a total of five core components as follows:
(a)   Lake Dianchi Basin Water Quality Recovery.  Investments would
support the Dianchi Environmental Action Plan (DEAP), and would
include control of point and nonpoint pollution in the Dianchi Lake Basin
through improved catchment management, wastewater and stormwater
collection, treatment and disposal; improved collection, transport and
disposal of municipal solid waste and nightsoil; remediation of polluting
practices causing lake eutrophication; measures to strengthen Dianchi
Lake basin management and safeguard the long term quality of water
resources; and improvements to basin water resource allocation, to ensure
that the highest quality water is used for the most quality-sensitive uses,
such as for potable supply. The latter would be supported through
investments in upgraded trunk and distribution water mains, water
treatment, and associated actions and facilities to protect and increase the
water supply in Kunming;                        $199.6 million
(b)   Industrial Pollution Control. Direct investment financial support would
be provided to abate industrial pollution from enterprises identified in the
DEAP as most threatening to Dianchi Lake basin resources. Enterprises
have been ranked according to environmental impact, using rankings to be
confirmed during appraisal. The cleanup would focus on two fertilizer
plants, found to be the top-ranked industrial polluters in terms of
phosphorus load to Dianchi Lake. Support to abate pollution from lower-
ranked enterprises requiring smaller investments would be through an
Environmental Pollution Control Fund Subloans (EPCSL) Facility;
$34.1 million
(c)   Provincial Urban Environmental Services. Investments in catchment
management, wastewater and stormwater collection and treatment systems
for the protection of Gejiu Lake and Qujing's Nanpanjiang River; selective



-127 -                     ANNEX7
investments in water supply system extensions in Qujing; and improved
collection, transport and disposal of municipal solid waste and nightsoil in
Qujing;                                          $48.6 million
(d)   Environmental and Water Quality Monitoring. Support for upgrading
and modernizing environmental management and water quality
information and monitoring systems.               $6.2 million
(e)   Institutional Development, Training and Construction Supervision.
Strengthening provincial, municipal, project, and financial management;
construction supervision services; training in utility operations; updating
of city environmental action plans and feasibility studies; and future
project preparation.                             $19.1 million
TOTAL ESTIMATED PROJECT COST                    $307.6 million
12.   The financial, urban environment service delivery and utility management policy
initiatives would concentrate on (a) operational efficiency improvements; (b) resource
mobilization and cost recovery measures; (c) long term financial and economic
sustainability underpinning; and (d) industrial pollution reduction through focused time-
bound Action Plans with respect to pollution from paper, steel and pesticides. The
institutional development and policy strengthening component [para. 3.2(e)] would
enhance policies and operational experience in environmental protection and water,
wastewater, municipal solid waste and nightsoil management. Support to these initiatives
has been grouped into a number of main composite packages for the various executing
agencies, covering institutional development, financial management improvement,
construction supervision and training. The component would also support provincial and
sector city agencies, master planning, future project preparation, and other programs of
assistance to be defined.
Baseline Data
13.   General. Yunnan is the third poorest province in China. The economy of
Yunnan is dominated by primary production, principally agriculture, with manufacturing
accounting for a relatively small proportion of output. Kunming is a substantial industrial
center, dominating the provincial economy. There is evidence of a shift toward tertiary
sector activities in recent years.
14.   An important feature of industry in both Yunnan and Kunming is the dominance
of state-owned enterprises, principally at provincial and municipal level. These account
for about 75 percent of gross output, compared with a corresponding figure of 43 percent
for China as a whole. This is an important factor in environmental terms, because many
are loss-making, in some cases, such as fertilizer production, as a consequence of Central
Government controls on output prices. As a result many appear to be unable to fund
pollution control measures.



-128 -                     ANNEX7
15.   Qujing is the second largest city in Yunnan, with a population of 161,500, and is
located some 156 km to the northeast of Kunming. Industry has expanded rapidly in
recent years and Qujing is one of the six cities in Yunnan designated for high short-term
economic and population growth (the population is expected to more than double by
2010). Industrial activity is based on cigarette production, nonferrous metallurgy,
chemicals and automobile manufacture. Qujing is well located on the road and rail
networks for ready access both to Kunming and to other provinces.
16.   Gejiu is located to the south of Kunming in a mountainous area in the
southernmost part of Yunnan. The city has grown largely around the development of the
tin mining and processing industries. The population of Gejiu city is currently 134,000,
with a further 80,000 in four satellite towns that house most of the major industries.
17.   Climate. Yunnan has a northern subtropical monsoon climate, with extremes of
temperature moderated by altitude and latitude. Precipitation is plentiful (ranging
between 800 and 1,000 mm/year) and the province as a whole is relatively water rich,
with per capita runoff approaching 2.5 times the national average. The average annual
temperature in the Dianchi Lake basin is 14.70. There is a pronounced wet season, from
May to September, during which more than 70 percent of the rainfall occurs. However,
regional differences in the balance between rainfall and evaporation and substantial
temporal variability in rainfall result in areas with very limited water resources. The
Dianchi Lake basin is one such area. Evaporation from land surfaces in the basin is high,
in the order of 2,000 mm. The principal wind direction is southwest, with mean wind
speeds in the range 2.2 to 3 m/sec.
18.   Water Resources. The water resources of the Dianchi Lake basin are severely
limited, with per capita runoff only 3 percent of the world and 5 percent of national mean
levels. Renewable water resources are currently average 31 1 mi3 per capita, but as a result
of population growth this will fall in 2020 to 200 m3 on average and as low as 90 m3 in a
drought year. The latter figure would barely be sufficient to support domestic water
demand alone.
19.   At present water demands (agricultural, industrial and domestic) in the Dianchi
Lake basin exceed supply by between 177 Mm3/year and 578 Mm3/year in average and
dry (20 percentile) years respectively. This supply imbalance is corrected by recycling of
water, mainly the discharges of untreated wastewater from Kunming to the Dianchi Lake.
20.   As a result of the temporal variations in rainfall water, supply depends heavily
upon reservoir storage. The principal supply source for Kunming is the Songhuaba
Reservoir, which has the dual roles of water supply and flood protection. The mean
annual runoff and total storage volume of Songhuaba Reservoir are 198 Mm3 and
229 Mm3, respectively. However, because of flood control requirements (1 in 100-year
flood), the effective storage available for water supply is only 100 Mm3, which gives a
mean annual yield of 130 Mm3 (350,000 m3/d) with an assurance rate of 95 percent. In



-129 -                      ANNEX7
practice the reservoir is operated at a level 1.5 m below the operating rule curve because
farmers have not received compensation payments for the loss of land involved.
21.   The reservoir currently supplies water for agricultural as well as municipal use. It
is proposed in future to substitute Dianchi Lake water for use in agriculture to conserve
the better quality Songhuaba water for potable supply.
22.   The majority of the water demand in Kunming is met by abstractions from
Dianchi Lake, amounting to 480,000 m3/d. The Dianchi Lake has an average depth of
approximately 4.5 m and the overall storage volume is 1,250 Mm3. However, the
effective storage is substantially less than this figure because of the flood control
requirements. The lake receives runoff from urban rivers in Kunming, which receive
overspill from Songhuaba Reservoir, but in the dry season are largely open sewers, and
from rivers traversing the agricultural areas to the east and south of the lake.
23.   Flood protection is achieved by the discharge of water from the lake via the sole
outlet at Haikou, at the southwest of the lake, to the Tanglangchuan river. The hydraulic
capacity of the Tanglangchuan is limited, and a further flood alleviation measure, the
Xiyuan Tunnel at the northeast of the lake, has been constructed to increase the rate of
discharge of floodwater. In spite of these and other measures aimed at increasing the
effective storage in Dianchi Lake, the increased water demands are such that they can
only be met by ever increasing recycling of Kunming municipal wastewater.
24.   The principal watercourse in Qujing municipality is the Nanpanjiang, which
provides the majority of the water supply to Qujing via three reservoirs on its tributaries.
The remainder is obtained from groundwater. These resources are insufficient to meet
future demands, for which it will be necessary to import water from distant reservoirs.
25.   Gejiu derives water supplies from a combination of three reservoirs to the south of
the City and from groundwater and springs. Most of industry is supplied from Gejiu
Lake, an artificial lake constructed in 1955 with a volume of about 4 Mm3 largely for
flood protection. The lake has for many years received discharges of urban wastewater,
soil eroded from mining areas above the city and tailings from tin processing. As a result
there has been a substantial reduction in its effective volume and self-purification
capacity. Between 1961 and 1986 sediments were dredged from the lake, which
maintained a reasonable storage capacity.
26.   During the dry season, wastewater discharges to the lake constitute about two-
thirds of the available water supply. Water abstraction by industry in the dry season in
the order of 3 Mm3, and therefore this inflow of wastewater, or a suitable replacement, is
crucial to the water balance of the lake if abstractions are to continue.
27.   Water Quality. The Chinese water quality classification is based on five levels
of beneficial water use, as shown in Table 1.



- 130-                     ANNEX7
TABLE 1: CHINA: WATER QUALITY CLASSIFICATION SYSTEM
Classification               Description of Water Use Function
I        National Nature Protection Area
II        Domestic Potable Water Source-Grade I protection area, precious
fish and fish culture zone
III       Domestic Potable Water Source-Grade II protection area, general
fish and bathing areas
IV        Industrial Water Use and Recreation
V         Agricultural and general amenity use
28.   Water quality in Songhuaba Reservoir is currently classified as Class II, but the
limited data available indicate increasing nutrient concentrations in the reservoir and
hence progressive eutrophication. Although the reservoir catchment was designated a
Water Protection Zone in 1981 but continuing lack of control has resulted in increasing
farming and rural township expansion in the area.
29.   The Dianchi Lake is divided into two parts, the Caohai (inner lake) and the much
larger Waihai (outer lake), which accounts for about 97 percent of the lake. The Caohai
and the Waihai are physically separated by a barrage completed in 1996. The municipal
and industrial wastewater from Kunming is discharged, mainly untreated into both the
Caohai and Waihai.
30.   There are currently only two operational sewage treatment works in Kunming,
treating a flow of 155,000 m3/d, which discharges ultimately to the Caohai. A further two
plants are under construction, with a total treatment capacity of 260,000 m3/d, of which
210,000 m3/d will be discharged to the Waihai. Treatment processes at all sewage
treatment works incorporate partial removal of both nitrogen and phosphorus.
31.   The Caohai is particularly polluted, with water quality failing to comply even with
Class V standards. In particular the concentrations of nitrogen and phosphorus are high,
typically up to 7.5 and 0.9 mg/l respectively. Water quality in the Waihai has also
deteriorated in recent years, and nitrogen and phosphorus concentrations are again high,
averaging 1.5 and 0.14 mg/l respectively in 1995.
32.   Water is abstracted from the Waihai for treatment at two water treatment plants.
However, poor water quality in terms of algal growths resulting from the high nutrient
concentrations are causing severe treatment difficulties. In particular, one water intake
close to the boundary with the Caohai has been closed and additional treatment processes,
using ozone and activated carbon are proposed for the treatment works treating water
from the Waihai.



-131-                      ANNEX7
33.   Phosphorus has been identified as the growth-limiting nutrient and therefore the
principal focus of nutrient load reduction in wastewater treatment.  The potential
phosphorus loads to both the Caohai and Waihai are shown in Table 2.
TABLE 2: PHOSPHORUS LOADS TO DIANCHI LAKE (1995)
Source                                    Phosphorus Loads (t/year)
Caohai               Waihai
Kunming wastewater                       126                  210
Kunyang Fertilizer                                             57
Kunming Fertilizer                                             36
Other industry                            24                    11
Chenggong wastewater                                            6
Jinning wastewater                                              9
Total point source                    150                   329
Runoff                                    57                  108
Atmosphere                                 2                   55
Rural population                          1 5                  65
Total nonpoint source                  74                   228
TOTAL                                 224                   557
34.   Table 2 shows that the loads are in both cases dominated by point source
discharges of urban and industrial wastewater. Moreover, the data of loads from
nonpoint sources are based on very limited measurements and are considered to be
overestimates.
35.   Discharges to the Caohai have in the past entered the Waihai, and therefore the
load to the Waihai would have been greater than that indicated in Table 2. However, the
construction of the barrage and the Xiyuan tunnel has now allowed the diversion in dry
weather of water from the Caohai, principally urban wastewater, away from the Waihai to
the downstream Tanglangchuan river. In addition to the high nutrient levels in both the
Caohai and the Waihai there is concern also at discharges of organic matter from
municipal wastewater and toxins from industry.
36.   The Tanglangchuan river receives water discharged from the Waihai at Haikou
throughout the year, either for flood control purposes or to maintain a base flow in the dry
season. There is considerable exploitation of the Tanglangchuan for irrigated agriculture
and by industry. Water quality in the Tanglangchuan is poor as a result of discharges of
untreated industrial wastewater. Water is wastefully discharged from Dianchi Lake



-132 -                      ANNEX7
simply to provide dilution of this pollution to maintain even the present level of water
quality.
37.   Water quality in the Nanpanjiang upstream of Qujing and in the reservoirs serving
Qujing is generally good, typically meeting Class I standards. However, the Nanpanjiang
is seriously polluted by discharges of municipal wastewater from Qujing and of industrial
wastewater, including discharges from two fertilizer plants and a paper mill. As a result
water quality has declined to Class V, principally in terms of high chemical oxygen
demand (COD) and ammonia concentrations. The stated objective is to improve water
quality to Class IV by 2000 and to Class III by 2010.
38.   Gejiu Lake has been seriously polluted by the large discharges of municipal and
industrial wastewater, with water quality failing to meet even Class V standards. Even
during cooler months quiescent areas appear black and septic. Sediments are highly
contaminated with metals (arsenic, lead and cadmium) discharged from tin mining and
related activities, although there have been dramatic reductions in toxic metal discharges
over the past 20 years. The objective for Gejiu Lake is to improve water quality to
Class IV, sufficient to support industrial water use and recreation.
39.   The principal cause of the deteriorating water quality in all cases is the virtual
absence of effective wastewater treatment, particularly in the case of industry. The
primary instrument in China for control of industrial pollution is the effluent fee system,
but the fees charged are considerably below levels necessary to have the required
incentive effect in terms of wastewater treatment. Moreover, there is only partial
collection of fees, particularly in Kunming, where the collection rate is only of the order
of 20 percent.
40.   Terrestrial and Aquatic Ecology. Approximately 22 percent of the Dianchi
Lake basin is occupied by cropped land and about 37 percent by forests. The forests
consist principally of subtropical evergreen broad-leafed species but also include areas of
deciduous broad leafed forest, temperate pine forest, shrub grassland, bamboo forest and
grassland. Eucalyptus globulus has been introduced as a plantation species and occurs
throughout the basin.
41.   There has been considerable degradation of the forest as a result of use of wood as
a fuel and of problems of forest management control and land allocation. Nevertheless
the proportion of the basin area accounted for by forest cover remains the same as that in
1975.
42.   Fish species diversity in Dianchi Lake declined between the late 1950s and 1985
from 53 to 27 as a result of protective dike construction and land reclamation. These
activities destroyed the shallow water areas where fish would normally breed and have
nursery grounds. Of the surviving 32 species, only 6 are now of commercial importance,
but the total annual yield has declined much less dramatically than species numbers.
Dianchi Lake remains the largest fish producer in Yunnan.



-133 -                       ANNEX 7
43.   The dike construction and land reclamation activities have contributed to the
degradation of the Dianchi Lake in respect of widespread destruction of its littoral zone
and of aquatic macrophytes. In addition deforestation contributed to the phosphorus
loading to the lake as a result of soil erosion, which has also reduced the lake volume.
The problems of the Dianchi Lake therefore derive both from the increase in the loads of
phosphorus to the lake and from the reduction in its ability to assimilate phosphorus as a
result of the disruption of its natural ecology.
44.   Waste Management. Overall responsibility for the collection and disposal of
municipal and commercial wastes and street sweepings rests with the City Appearance
Committees, under the leadership and guidance of the Municipal Environmental
Sanitation Sections. Collection of solid wastes is generally effective, although in some
areas unsightly large quantities of waste line roadsides and river banks, where it is left to
decompose for subsequent use as compost.
45.   Disposal of solid wastes has traditionally been to land, principally to numerous
large and small open dumps, and not using acceptable standards of sanitary landfill. Two
major sites serving Kunming have received more than 2 million tons over the past
10 years. The open dumps are unhygienic and unsatisfactory and are not registered or
monitored. Since the dumps are neither lined nor covered, there are potential problems of
leachate and windblown pollution. There is also particular concern over the uncontrolled
disposal of toxic wastes.
46.   Quantities of municipal solid waste on a per capita basis are declining in mass
terms as a result of the change from coal to gas as a fuel. As a result the organic content
is higher. There are informal recycling activities, but the proportion of waste recycled is
unknown. The decline in the use of nightsoil by farmers in favor of chemical fertilizers
has resulted in the disposal of increasing quantities of nightsoil with the solid waste
streain.
Consideration of Alternatives
47.   In most cases the approaches adopted for the various project components
represent the only realistic option in terms of resource availability or achievement of the
required environmental objective. Where multiple options were available, the least-cost
solution achieving the required objective was selected.
48.   In the case of rehabilitation of the Dianchi Lake with respect to its trophic state, it
would not be possible within a realistic timeframe to enhance sufficiently the ability of
the lake to assimilate phosphorus. The only realistic approach to improving water quality
in the immediate term is therefore to reduce the phosphorus loads discharged to the lake.
49.   The proposed Kunming wastewater treatment works processes are based on
biological treatment incorporating nitrogen and phosphorus removal in addition to
achieving a high degree of removal of organic matter. The alternative of a totally
chemically based treatment system would not readily be able to achieve all the required



-134-                      ANNEX7
discharge standards, particularly in respect of removal of nitrogen and organic matter.
Further removal of nutrients from the Waihai by diversion of treated wastewater to the
Caohai and thence via the Xiyuan tunnel was rejected in view of water resource
limitations on the Waihai. Reducing the phosphorus loads in domestic wastewater by
enforcing the use of phosphate-free detergents would not of itself reduce loads by a
sufficient degree to obviate the need for phosphorus removal in wastewater treatment.
Since phosphorus removal in treatment is not directly dependent upon concentration in
the raw wastewater, a ban on phosphate in detergents would not be appropriate.
50.   Sanitary landfill was preferred to other waste disposal options in all cases.
Incineration would not be appropriate in view of the low heat value of the wastes,
resulting from either a high inert ash content, arising from domestic use of coal as a fuel,
or from a high moisture content. Composting was rejected in view of concerns over
adequacy and stability of product market outlets. Moreover, landfill would be required in
any event for reject materials from other options.
Environmental Benefits
51.   The balance of the project is substantially positive, and there are considerable
environmental benefits for the Dianchi Lake basin and for the cities of Qujing and Gejiu.
These benefits are discussed in detail below.
52.   Dianchi Lake Basin. The primary focus of the sewage treatment works and
industrial pollution control projects is the reduction of the point source loads of nutrients,
principally phosphorus, to the Dianchi Lake, particularly the Waihai. The proposed
municipal sewage treatment works, Nos. 4b and 5 and extension to No. 1, are designed to
achieve the National Integrated Discharge Standard, in particular a phosphorus
concentration in the treated wastewater of not more than 1 mg/l. On this basis they will
achieve reductions in phosphorus loads of between 60 and 80 percent, depending upon
initial phosphorus concentrations which are a function of the relative contribution of
industrial wastewater. Removal of organic matter from wastewater will be in the order of
90 percent.
53.  In addition the associated sewerage systems will collect urban wastewater that
currently discharges to the rivers that traverse Kunming. As a result there will be a
substantial improvement in water quality in these rivers.
54.   The water supply and treatment components will not of themselves have a direct
benefit on water quality in Dianchi Lake. They will, however, permit a more optimal use
of the water resources in the basin in that they will increase the use of the higher quality
water from Songhuaba Reservoir for domestic potable supply.
55.   The pollution control proposals for the two fertilizer factories, Kunyang
Phosphorus Fertilizer and Kunming Chemical Fertilizer, are based on a combination of
process upgrading, water conservation and wastewater treatment. The phosphorus load



-135 -                      ANNEX7
from the former factory will be reduced by 54.5 t/year (98 percent) and from the latter by
35 t/year (97 percent).
56.   Wastewater treatment for the rural townships of Chenggong and Jinning would
also be based on biological treatment including removal of nitrogen and phosphorus. The
treatment proposed will reduce the phosphorus loads to the Waihai from these sources by
in the order of 75 percent.
57.   The overall benefit of the wastewater treatment components in terms of reductions
in phosphorus loads to the Waihai is shown in Table 3. The loads referred to in the table
relate to conditions obtaining in the year 2010, the time horizon of the designs for all
project components.
58.   It will be seen from Table 3 that the prospective phosphorus loads that would be
generated in 2010 represent a very substantial increase, some 40 percent over the 1995
case. The increase is principally as a result of population growth and increased living
standards, including the progressive abandonment of the nightsoil system.
59.   The existing and proposed interventions (principally wastewater treatment plants
Nos. 2 and 4a, the former constructed with assistance from bilateral funding) could
potentially achieve a significant reduction in phosphorus load.  However, their
effectiveness is to some degree reduced by deficiencies in sewerage systems and therefore
degree of collection of wastewater for treatment. The values in Table 3 for the 2010 case
with the existing and proposed interventions assumes that, until the sewerage system
improvements are implemented, the treatment works will continue to treat polluted water
drawn from the adjacent rivers. These rivers are in any event open sewers in the dry
season, and it has been assumed that 25 percent of the annual phosphorus load of the
sewage drawn from the rivers will escape in storm water during the wet season.
60.   The wastewater treatment plant designs are based on achieving a residual
phosphorus concentration of not more than 1 mg/l, and therefore the average
concentration would be significantly lower than I mg/l. Table 3 shows the estimates of
the phosphorus loads discharged which based on a residual concentration in the treated
wastewater of 0.5 mg/l, which relates more closely to the likely average concentration.
61.   Implementation of the wastewater collection and treatment components of the
project, together with the pollution control proposals for the two fertilizer plants, reduce
the 2010 total phosphorus load by about 50 percent, to a level some 33 percent below the
1995 level. Moreover, it is considered that this simple evaluation based on total
phosphorus understates the potential effectiveness of the proposals.  Much of the
phosphorus associated with the nonpoint sources is in the form of insoluble particulates
and therefore not readily available for algal consumption. It has conservatively been
estimated that 70 percent of the phosphorus from nonpoint sources is insoluble. When
this factor is taken into account, the potential load reduction achieved by the project is
estimated to be 46 percent relative to the 1995 level.

-137 -                      ANNEX 7
62.   The corresponding reductions to the Caohai are shown also in Table 3, and a
similar situation obtains in respect of the need for sewerage system enhancement to
ensure that the proposed wastewater treatment plants, Nos. 1 and 3, can be effective in
reducing phosphorus loads. Again the implementation of the project proposals is
estimated to reduce the load of phosphorus to the Caohai to significantly below the 1995
case.
63.   In view of these substantial reductions in phosphorus load it would be expected
that there would be a significant reduction in phosphorus concentrations in the Waihai,
and therefore a corresponding reduction in the water treatment problems relative to those
currently experienced. In practice the degree and rate of recovery will to a certain extent
be limited by the reduced ability of the Waihai to assimilate phosphorus as a consequence
of the disruption of the natural ecology. The relationship between phosphorus loads to
the Waihai and the concentration of phosphorus on an annual average basis has been
evaluated using a mathematical model that simulates the effect of various processes
occurring in the lake (e.g., sedimentation, uptake by fish). The calculated phosphorus
concentrations for the period 1995 to 2015 resulting from the implementation of YEP and
for the Do Nothing More cases, based on implementation only of the wastewater
treatment interventions already planned are shown in Figure 1.
64.   It is apparent from Figure 1 that implementation of YEP will sustain phosphorus
concentrations in the Waihai at, or around, present levels and will therefore allow
continued use for water supply, albeit with extensive treatment such as ozone and
activated carbon. Failure to implement YEP will result in increases in phosphorus
concentrations to levels where even advanced treatment would become extremely
problematic and it would be necessary to substitute Dianchi Lake by water imported from
distant catchments. Figure I also shows that, even if it were possible to prevent all
discharges of phosphorus, of whatever source, to the Waihai, it would be in the order of
10 years before phosphorus concentrations returned to historic low levels.
65.   The two sanitary landfill sites proposed for Kunming will provide capacity for the
environmentally acceptable disposal of solid wastes from Kunming for approximately
20 years, and thereby reduce the potential impact on groundwater and surface waters
from  the present uncontrolled tipping of waste.  There will be corresponding
improvements in the efficiency of collection and transport, which will reduce the
environmental degradation and visual pollution arising from unauthorized dumping of
waste in urban areas.
Qujing
66.   The water supply component for Qujing will alleviate raw water shortages,
particularly in dry years when as low as only 60 percent of urban demand has been met.
The wastewater treatment plant, with a capacity of 100,000 m3/d, provides only partial
treatment in the immediate term, based on chemically assisted primary sedimentation. It



-138-                      ANNEX7
will nevertheless remove a substantial pollution load, but the longer-tern water quality
objectives for the Nanpanjiang will require also control of industrial wastewater.

67.   The wastes management proposals will provide environmentally sound landfill
disposal facilities for wastes from Qujing, replacing the existing largely uncontrolled
dump sites which are known to be polluting groundwater. The waste collection
improvements, including transfer stations, will improve collection efficiency and reduce
the degree of flytipping.
Gejiu
68.   The wastewater and flood protection proposals for Gejiu, in addition to improving
discharges of water from Gejiu lake, will collect wastewater currently discharged to the
lake and thereby reduce the pollution load that the lake receives. The wastewater
discharged to the Zhadian River will provide additional water for agricultural use.
Impacts and Mitigation
69.   Notwithstanding the very substantial positive balance of environmental impact of
the project, there are nevertheless some adverse impacts that require mitigation. These
impacts occur both in construction and operational phases of the project components.
The principal impacts and the proposed mitigation measures are described in detail
below; a summary of impacts and mitigations is given in Table 4.

Land Acquisition
70.     With the exception only of the pollution control projects for the fertilizer factories
all components require land acquisition on either a temporary or permanent basis. The
total permanent land acquisition requirement is 224 ha, with 163 ha in the Dianchi Lake
basin, 59.7 ha in Qujing and 1.8 ha in Gejiu respectively. The areas of land to be
acquired are dominated by the landfill site requirements, which account for 123 ha for
Kunming, of which 67 ha for one site has already been acquired, and 45.7 ha for Qujing.
Resettlement
71.     All project components affect local residents or enterprises and their employees.
The total number of persons affected is 2,966, but there will not be a need for any
residential resettlement. A total of four enterprises will be affected, three in the extension
of WTP No. I and one in the extension of STW No. 1. A detailed Resettlement Action
Plan has been prepared, and resettlement is described in detail in Annex 6.
Wastewater Treatment Impacts
72.     The principal impact associated with wastewater treatment is the generation of
substantial quantities  of treatment residues  (sludge, grit and  screenings).   At full
development of wastewater treatment facilities in Kunming the total quantity of sludge
requiring disposal would be in the order of 200 t/d in the form of dewatered sludge cake.
Even where the quality of sludge would be acceptable for use in agriculture, it is unlikely
that there would be sufficient demand for use in agriculture to reduce this quantity
significantly. The primary disposal route will therefore be by landfill at the new sites
serving Kunming. Disposal of sludge from the wastewater treatment facilities at Qujing
and Gejiu will be for agricultural use where possible or by landfill.
73.     The other main impacts of wastewater treatment are odor and noise, principally
from aeration equipment. Problems of odor are minimized in most cases by the absence
of primary treatment and hence the need to process primary sludge. Odor will be further



-141-                        ANNEX7
minimized by rapid disposal of sludge to avoid storage and by providing greenbelt buffer
zones around the periphery of the sites. Noise impact will be minimized by plant design
to maximize distances from any sensitive receivers, and where necessary by acoustic
insulation of buildings.
Waste Disposal Impacts
74.   The major impacts from  landfill disposal of municipal waste will be the
generation of leachate and of landfill gas. At all sites groundwater will be protected by
provision of synthetic site liners and by collection of leachate. It is proposed to store
leachate for recirculation through the landfill, thereby minimizing both the quantity for
disposal and its polluting character.
75.   The location of the sites, a substantial distance from  significant centers of
population, is beneficial in terms of reducing odor nuisance. However, it also means that
it is unlikely that beneficial use of landfill gas for energy recovery will be economic.
Landfill gas will therefore be flared for safety reasons and for conversion of methane, a
potent greenhouse gas, to the less potent carbon dioxide.
Spoil Disposal
76.   Considerable quantities of excavation spoil will be generated during the
construction of all water and wastewater treatment plants, water transmission and
sewerage systems. In particular there will be substantial amounts of tunneling spoil from
the DuMu aqueduct in Qujing and the tunnel in Gejiu. A detailed spoil disposal plan will
be prepared, incorporating wherever possible the beneficial use of spoil in construction
activities or landscaping.
Industry Impacts
77.   The production upgrading proposals at the two fertilizer plants include in one case
new facilities for production of sulfuric acid from  sulfur.  Tail gases from  these
production facilities contain sulfur dioxide and acid aerosols and are to be discharged to
the atmosphere via tall (at least 45 m) stacks.  In the individual environmental
assessments for the two plants it was shown by calculation that the discharges would not
result in unacceptable ground-level ambient concentrations of the pollutants.
Other Construction Impacts
78.   A number of other impacts will arise as a result of construction activities,
including disruption to traffic and municipal services, temporary loss of farmland, noise
and dust. In all cases impact will be minimized by adherence to defined operational
procedures, liaison with the appropriate authorities and appropriate compensation where
necessary. For example, noise impact will be minimized by restrictions on hours of
working and dust from excavation and spoil transport by means of water sprays and by
vehicle washing, covering and controls on loading.
