-153 -                      ANNEX 12
ANNEX 12: INDUSTRIAL POLLUTION CONTROL
Introduction
1.    Yunnan's industrial growth of nearly 9 percent per year over the past decade has
been accompanied by increasing discharges of wastewater, solid waste, and air pollutants.
An increasingly strict environmental regulatory regime has held waste discharges below the
overall growth rate, but wastewater and air polluting discharges have still grown some 6
percent per year. Naturally, the impact of this growth varies with location, industrial
concentration and type of effluent. The candidate cities for the YEP have the largest
concentrations of industry, and some of the most highly polluting, in the province:
Kunming and Gejiu industry discharge to lakes with declining absorptive capacity and
Qujing industrial discharges to the Nanpanjiang River have already rendered that body
unusable for several kilometers below the city. Heavy metal and toxic organic chemical
discharges continue in all of those cities. Of the worst 100 polluting industries in Yunnan,
Kunming contains 27, the Gejiu/Kaiyuan area 19, and Qujing 16.
2.    In the province as a whole, the chemical industry remains the worst polluter,
responsible for 62 percent of all industrial water pollution and providing 30 of the top 100
polluters (22 for wastewater pollution, 8 for air pollution). Next comes the metallurgical
industry, with 14 percent of wastewater and 27 percent of air pollution, and 20 of the top
polluters. The pulp and paper industry ranks third, with 23 of the top polluters, all in
wastewater, and sugarcane processing next with 6 top polluters, again all in wastewater.
Other top polluters include a variety of thermal power plants and cement plants, with air
pollution being their main problem.
3.    Kunming's Lake Dianchi provides one measure of the relative impact of industrial
pollution. This lake has become eutrophic, with serious consequences for beneficial uses of
the lake. Phosphorus is the limiting nutrient in Dianchi, and annual available phosphorus
loads are judged to total some 522 tons, of which industry contributes 115 tons, or 22
percent. Of the 115 tons of available industrial phosphorus, two plants, those of the
Kunming Chemical Fertilizer Company and the Kunyang Phosphorus Fertilizer Company,
contribute three-fourths of the load. The other major sources of phosphorus include
domestic and animal wastes, carried directly or, along with other sources, by storm runoff
and precipitation. Heavy metal loads discharging to Lake Dianchi have been largely
controlled, but in the recent past have forced the temporary closing of water treatment
plants and residual loads in the lake sediment remain large.
4.    Analysis of pollution sources and abatement costs in Yunnan demonstrates the
relatively low cost of addressing some pollutants at source. Reduction of phosphorus, for
example, costs about Y 2.7 million per ton per year in capital costs at the Kunyang



-154 -                      ANNEX 12
Phosphorus Fertilizer Plant and Y 6 million per ton per year in capital costs in the planned
Kunming No. 5 municipal treatment plant. Where pollutants are toxic to the conventional
wastewater treatment processes used in Yunnan municipal plants, or bypass those plants,
source control cannot be avoided. The Industrial Pollution Control component of the
project addresses these industrial point sources.
5.    The IPC component relies on a three part strategy: (a) direct onlending to clean up
the two fertilizer plants that are major polluters; (b) lending for a number of smaller
pollution control investments through an Environmental Pollution Control Subloans
(EPCSL) Facility, to be capitalized at $10 million, utilizing $5 million of proceeds of the
Bank loan/IDA credit, and an equal amount of local currency; and (c) action plans for the
cleanup of the Kunming Steel General Company and the Yunfeng Paper Mill, financed
through non-Bank sources, as well as ceasing operations in the manufacture and handling of
pesticides and other toxic materials at the Kunming Agricultural Chemical Factory plant by
June 30, 1998, to protect municipal wastewater treatment facilities. This annex discusses in
turn each part of the strategy.
FERTILIZER PLANT FINANCING FOR POLLUTION ABATEMENT
6.    Two fertilizer plants in the Dianchi Basin-the Kunyang Phosphate Fertilizer
Factory and the Kunming Chemical Fertilizer Factory-between them contribute 16 percent
of all available phosphate discharged into Dianchi Lake, and 73 percent of the industrial
contribution. Existing wastewater treatment facilities at the factories lack adequate design
capacity to handle waste flows, and the equipment breaks down with such frequency that
treatment goals remain unmet. Kunyang Phosphate maintains a modest profit of Y 3
million per year, or a return of about 2 percent on capital, while the Kunming Chemical
Fertilizer Factory eamed about Y 1.5 million in 1995 on capital of Y 90 million. In a
government attempt to hold down agricultural input prices, the fertilizer industry remains
one of the few subject to strict output price controls, coupled with mandated production.
Consequently, losses in these industries are known as "policy losses" and are covered
through subsidies to the plants (the Kunming Factory profit came after a Y4 million subsidy
payment). Despite the subsidies, the plants lack adequate operation and maintenance funds,
which exacerbates operating pressures on the waste treatment systems. The proposed
investments in the plants would radically change their production processes and
intermediate outputs, allowing profitable operation despite output price restraints.
Kunyang Phosphate Fertilizer Factory
7.    Kunyang contributes 57 tons per year of phosphorus to Dianchi, approximately 90
percent of that in soluble, or available, form. The plant produces a variety of phosphate
fertilizers and phosphate-based food additives, in addition to producing sulfuric acid for use
in producing double superphosphate. Current production processes do not maximize water
reuse, wastewater treatment cannot meet flow requirements, and obsolete equipment with
high breakdown rates leads to frequent unplanned discharges of process water. The
proposed project would rebuild much of the plant to maximize water reuse and minimize



-155-                       ANNEX12
pollution, leading to smaller, more easily handled wastewater streams. Those wastewater
streams would then undergo treatment improved from that currently used.
8.    The wastewater minimization engineering, coupled with raw material changes, will
substantially reduced total pollutant loads. Wastewater flows will drop from 540 m /hour to
117 m3/hour, with suspended solids cut by more than 50 percent; fluorine dropping from
19.8 tons/year-to 7.1 tons/year; and phosphorus from 57 to 18.5 tons/year. The addition of
secondary treatment of that residual stream will further cut all of the above pollutants, with
phosphorus dropping below 1 ton/year.
9.    To achieve the above goal, the factory will switch from pyrite-based sulfuric acid
production to the use of imported sulfur as a raw material. This will eliminate the waste
slag, which itself caused pollution through rainfall runoff leaching out toxic materials and
will allow a water recirculation system that requires only 34 m3/hour in replacement water,
compared to the 193 m3/hour now needed. The concentration of sulfur dioxide (SO2) in
waste gas will also drop from 500 ppm to 350 ppm, due to the purer raw material.
10.   In another move, the factory will cease production of calcium phosphate and
magnesium phosphate, increasing production of a variety of refined phosphate products
used in the food industry. The former production line discharges about 60 m3/hour of
wastewater and 4 tons/hour of sludge, both of which will end with the new products. The
factory already has experience producing and marketing refined phosphate for the food
industry, and these higher value-added products will contribute to increased firm
profitability.
11.   The double superphosphate production line will be overhauled, replacing corroded
equipment, shifting to cooling technologies that minimize the chance for pollution of the
cooling water, and adding a closed water system that allows recapture of phosphorus in the
washing water. The totality of these measures will reduce wastewater discharge by an
additional 170 m3/hour. Similar renovation and upgrading, to include separation of cooling
and process water streams, will reduce discharge and pollution in the yellow phosphorus
line.
12.   The remaining wastewater will discharge to a treatment plant optimized for removal
of the constituents of the plant waste stream. A full description of the process may be found
in the project files.
13.   The above technical transformation will not only radically reduce the pollutant load
from the plant, it will also improve its profitability. The key to that improvement lies not in
the pollution reduction per se-for the factory has not, heretofore, been charged the full
pollution levy fee for its polluting output. Rather, the ability to shift to a higher value
product mix, through the food phosphate market not subject to government price controls,
will produce that result.
14.   As the above description suggests, the current plant has suffered from neglected
routine maintenance, which is crucial to pollution control. Plant managers recognize this



-156 -                       ANNEX 12
failing and have launched a campaign to reduce pollution in 1996 by 20 percent through
improved housekeeping. To assist in that effort, the plant has requested that the loan
include technical assistance in operation and maintenance of their renovated facilities,
including the wastewater treatment plant
15.   Major portions of the proposed investment promise no financial return for the plant,
but do offer major environmental gains. The financial internal rate of return (IRR) of the
combined investment totals 9.6%, assuming that the plant operates 300 days a year (83
percent utilization). Actual utilization currently reaches 330 days per year. The results are
robust to utilization (or sales) charges, with 60 percent utilization (216 days) yielding a 7
percent IRR. The debt service coverage ratio is a key indication for pollution abatement
projects of this type, and remains above 1.4 under all scenarios but the 60 percent utilization
case, where it would fall to 1.3 for a single year.
Kunming Chemical Fertilizer Factory
16.   Kunming Chemical Fertilizer contributes 36 tons per year of phosphorus to Dianchi,
approximately 90 percent of that in soluble, or available, form. The plant produces a variety
of fertilizers, including NPK granular fertilizer and monoammonium phosphate (MAP). As
in Kunyang, current production processes do not maximize water reuse, wastewater
treatment cannot meet flow requirements, and obsolete equipment with high breakdown
rates leads to frequent unplanned discharges of process water. The proposed project would
rebuild much of the plant to maximize water reuse and minimize pollution, leading to
smaller, more easily handled wastewater streams. Those wastewater streams would then
undergo improved treatment from that currently used.
17.   As with Kunyang, the renovation strategy relies on replacing corroded and obsolete
equipment, separating process water from cooling water, and where possible, reusing
process water after recovery of constituent chemicals. Wastewater minimization alone will
reduce wastewater discharges from 550 m3/hour to 59 m3/hour, and in the process reduce
suspended solid loads from 217 to 85 tons/year, phosphorus from 36 to 3.5 tons/year, and
NH3-N from 332 to 85 tons/year. Treatment of that wastewater stream will further reduce
suspended solids to 21 tons/year, phosphorus to less than 0.5 tons/year, and NH3-N to
I 1 tons/year.
18.   The ammonia production line desulfurization will switch from a liquid-phase
catalysis method to a tannin-based method that will eliminate 30 m /hour of a high
ammonia wastewater stream and suspended sulfur will be recovered, the cooling water will
be changed to an isolated, closed system, the cooling system redesigned for greater
efficiency, and corroded equipment replaced. The phosphoric acid and MAP workshops
will replace corroded equipment and add agitators to tanks to decrease precipitation and
thus the need to wash the tanks. In addition, the gypsum storage site will to redesigned to
minimize and capture leachate.
19.   A sulfuric acid production facility, using sulfur as a raw material, will be set up to
end the need to purchase sulfuric acid on the market. Intermittent availability of sulfliric



-157 -                      ANNEX 12
acid has forced the MAP production line itself to run intermittently, in the last year at only
about 25 percent of capacity. With every shutdown of that line, the equipment must be
washed and pipes flushed, resulting in substantial unplanned waste loads that cannot be
handled by the current treatment plant. The sulfuric acid plant will be designed to the
improved standard to be used in Kunyang, hence pollution from that source can be
minimized.
20.   As in Kunyang, the remaining wastewater will discharge to a treatment plant
optimized for removal of the constituents of the plant waste stream. A full description of
the process may be found in the project files. Kunming has requested loan support for a
two-year technical assistance program in operation and maintenance of their new and
renovated plant and equipment. This will help plant managers realize their goal of cleaner
production and sustainable gains from the investment.
21.   The above technical transformation will not only radically reduce the pollutant load
from the plant, it will also improve its profitability. For Kunming Chemical Fertilizer, the
key to profitability lies in the new sulfuric acid plant, which will provide a reliable and less
expensive sulfuric acid supply to support normal production in the MAP facility. The
sulfuric acid plant will also produce a surplus that can be sold into a market free of
government price controls.
22.   The proposed investment will allow a substantial increase in the utilization of
existing plant and equipment, and for that reason produces a high internal rate of return
(IRR) of 93 percent at the base utilization rate of 83 percent (300 days/year). The results are
robust to utilization or sales charges, still reaching 26 percent if utilization falls to 60
percent. Similarly, substantial increases in input prices can be absorbed. The projected
debt service ratio remains above 1.4 under the tested utilization rates and price changes.
ENVIRONMENTAL POLLUTION CONTROL
23.   In addition to the two major contributors to Dianchi Lake pollution that will be
addressed directly through the project, a large number of other polluters also need technical
and financial assistance to bring their discharges within the permitted loads. As one means
of providing such assistance, a 1982 Chinese environmental law provided for the
establishment of provincial, municipal, and county environment funds financed by pollution
fees. These originally provided subsidies and grants for pollution reduction investments in
industrial and municipal pollution reduction projects, and for EPB environmental
monitoring and enforcement. A 1988 amendment to the law stipulated that up to 30 percent
of the fund income was to be loaned, rather than granted, to enterprises. Bank Group-
financed projects in Hubei, Jiangsu, Liaoning, Tianjin, Zhejiang, and the Medium-Sized
Cities project, have supported improved environmental fund project identification and
management.



-158 -                      ANNEX 12
Pollution Fee Collection and Use in Yunnan Province
24.   In Yunnan, municipal and county EPBs collect pollution levy fees, while the
provincial, municipal and county EPBs jointly plan fee collection and utilization. The
finance bureau at each level of government holds the revenue collected, and disburses it via
local branches of the People's Construction Bank of China according to a fund allocation
plan provided by the EPBs.
25.   Fee rates have been established for wastewater discharges, waste gas emissions,
solid waste dumping and noise pollution. Fee collections rose from Y 38 million in 1992 to
over Y 52 million in 1994, with over 70 percent of the collected fees used for industrial
pollution control and the remainder financing the EPBs' environmental monitoring and
enforcement work. Had all fees been collected to stipulated levels, actual collections would
have been much higher. A total of 4,815 industries paid fees in 1994, with the top 100
polluters paying about 40 percent of all levies collected, but even the top polluters pay only
about half of what the regulations stipulate. Township and village industries pay an even
smaller proportion of theoretical levies. The relatively low collection rates are common
across China, for Yunnan shares with other provinces a reluctance to shut down highly
polluting but marginally profitable, or even loss-making, enterprises.  Instead, local
governments seek production and marketing opportunities for these plants designed to both
restore their profitability and cut pollution. Such opportunities exist, as shown with the
fertilizer plants. The environment funds provide a source of capital for other such
investments.
26.   Bank Experience in China with Environmental Funds. China still has limited
experience with environmental loan funds.  The Bank Group has supported the
establishment of such funds under six different projects and three of them have been in
operation for at least one year. Compared to domestically financed funds, the Bank Group-
assisted funds have been found to have the following advantages:
(a)   they typically provide a stronger framework for management, including
organization, staffing and operation regulations, and consciously seek links
with other environmental protection management measures such as the
promotion of pollution prevention programs;
(b)   they institute rigorous requirements and procedures for project preparation
and appraisal, ensuring a higher project success rate; and
(c)   they train staff of the fund offices in the technical and financial areas.
27.   One disadvantage of Bank-supported funds is that the more rigorous appraisal
procedures have been expensive compared to the size of the typical loan, which has been
under $1 million. Due regard needs to be given to modifications of the appraisal procedures
to bring down costs while maintaining quality.



-159 -                      ANNEX 12
Environmental Pollution Control Subloans
28.   The purpose of EPCSL is to strengthen the existing Yunnan pollution prevention
program with a technically and institutionally sustainable financing mechanism. The
subloans would primarily assist small-scale industrial enterprises within the Dianchi basin
to control pollution through waste minimization measures, modifications or renovations to
existing plants to reduce waste outputs, or to recycle waste generated during production.
Industrial upgrading projects designed to reduce the amount of pollutant discharge and to
save natural resources and energy, commercial waste recovery services and centralized
treatment plants would also be considered by the Fund. The Yunnan Environmental
Protection Bureau (YEPB) has identified a priority list of Dianchi basin enterprises, and
others among the 100 most polluting enterprises, to receive preferential access to EPCSL.
29.   Organization and Financing of EPCSL. EPCSL would be a self-accounting
government facility, with initial capital equivalent of Y 84 million. The Bank Loan/IDA
Credit would provide $5 million and Yunnan Province would contribute an equivalent sum
(no less than Y 14 million for each of the first three years). While the size of the Bank/IDA
contribution has been kept small, the justification for Bank/IDA support for the facility
derives from, first, the advantages listed above.
30.   YPG has designated the Yunnan Finance Bureau (YFB) to administer EPCSL.
YEPB has prepared draft operations regulations for EPCSL, setting out the criteria for
subloans processing and approvals, the rules and regulations for management, and defining
the terms and conditions for loans. Disbursement against this component can begin only
after selection of a domestic bank (agent bank) to act as the agent in handling loan funds
and the signing of an agreement, acceptable to the Bank/IDA, between that domestic bank
and YP, laying out the agency agreement. The EPCSL Steering Committee would
determine the institutional structure and staff composition of the EPCSL Management
Office and approve its annual work plan and budget.
31.   The EPCSL Fund Management Office (SLMO) would have up to 6 staff, to be
recruited during a three-year period, including specialists in environmental science and
technology, industrial processing, economics and finance, and administration. The director
would be appointed by the Steering Committee, and other staff would be locally recruited.
The main role of SLMO would be in investment project planning, coordination between
borrower and agent bank, review of project feasibility and supervision of project
implementation. Financial appraisal will be handled within SLMO, while technical
appraisal will rely on consultants for industry-specific expertise.
32.   Project Selection. Each application would be first reviewed by the local industrial
bureau and planning commission, and approved by the concerned local EPB to ensure
consistency with the municipal environmental planning. On that basis, YEPB will lead an
appraisal that looks particularly at the appropriateness of the investment, given available
technology and the input and output markets; the quality of enterprise management as
demonstrated in their current operating environment; the suitability of the pollution



-160 -                     ANNEX 12
abatement measures proposed to the effluents produced in the enterprise; and the design of
easily maintained and operated systems well within the technical competence of enterprise
personnel.
Project Management Procedures
33.   EPCSL operating procedures will include the following:
(a)   The SLMO would issue directives and solicitations for project proposals to
the priority area EPBs and planning commissions;
(b)   With consent from the local industrial bureau, the enterprise would submit a
loan application to its EPB and planning commission;
(c)   With EPB and planning commission concurrence, the enterprise would
prepare a feasibility study report, including a financial proposal for
investment and an environmental impact assessment;
(d)   After the project has been successfully appraised, SLMO would submit the
appraisal report and proposed loan conditions to the World Bank Group for
no-objection. The Bank/IDA would provide ex-ante review of the first three
subloan projects and thereafter each subloan of over $300,000, with all other
subloans subject to ex-post review;
(e)   The EPCSL Steering Committee would be responsible for the final approval
of subloan projects. After approval, SLMO would confirm the loan to the
enterprise, any cofinancing agencies and the agent bank;
(f)   SLMO would supervise the implementation of the project;
(g)   After satisfying itself as to the quality of a commissioned project, the
relevant bureau would issue a Certificate of Examination and Acceptance to
the enterprise;
(h)   The bureau in charge would submit examination and acceptance documents
for the project to SLMO; and
(i)   SLMO would separately assess whether the project meets the design
document requirements. If it does not, SLMO would arrange for appropriate
departments to intervene, seeking necessary amendments to the project.
Project Appraisal
34.   For the technical appraisal of individual projects, the general criteria to be
considered are: (a) the project's impact on the ambient environrent; (b) compliance with
environmental regulations and standards; and (c) technical feasibility and identification of
the least-cost option.



-161-                      ANNEX 12
35.   The economic appraisal must show that the enterprise is economically viable.
36.   The financial appraisal must demonstrate that the enterprise, as a whole, would be
financially viable, including meeting a debt service ratio larger than 1.4 after the proposed
project reaches full production capacity. For joint waste treatment facilities, a financing
plan for the operation and maintenance of the facility would be required.
Project Financing Procedures Between Agent Bank and Borrower
37.   Once a subloan application is approved by the EPCSL Steering Conmnittee, the
agent bank would execute the loan according to procedures described below:
(a)   The agent would confirm the project financing plan with the borrower, in
accordance with the loan agreement signed between Yunnan Province (YP)
and the enterprise;
(b)   The project unit would open an account with the agent;
(c)   The agent would disburse funds upon receipt of statement of expenditure, in
the form of invoice or letter of credit. Normally, disbursement would be
made once a month;
(d)   The agent would supervise the fund's use by the project unit. If project
funds are found to have been used for any purpose other than the approved
project, the agent would refuse further disbursement and notify SLMO;
(e)   The agent would report to YFB quarterly on the financial status of loans
made to borrowers. The agent would also report emerging issues and make
suggestions for improvement as needed;
(f)   At project completion, the agent would finalize the financial accounting;
(g)   If projects fail to meet project date or design criteria, the agent shall notify
SLMO; and
(h)   The agent would assist SLMO in the collection of loan principal, interest,
late payment penalties and fines.
Disbursement would be made through the agent from the account for EPCSL in the Yunnan
Finance Bureau. EPCSL would be audited annually, to include a management letter.
Loans
38.   The loan for any project would be limited to 100 percent of foreign exchange costs,
or 50 percent of total project costs (excluding land), whichever is higher. Total project size
would not normally exceed $1 million. Loan maturity would be three to five years, with up
to two years of grace. YFB would bear the subloan credit risk, and the enterprise the



-162 -                     ANNEX 12
foreign exchange risk. The subloan interest rate would be no less than the standard interest
rate for LIBOR-based US dollar single currency loans plus a spread of 1.5 percent per year
and a commitment charge of 0.75 percent. Repayments to EPCSL would be recycled for
additional subprojects to the extent funds are not needed for repayment of the Bank/IDA
loan.
Institutional Development and Equipment Needs
39.   The Subloans Management Office personnel would undergo an extensive training
program.  Training courses would include economic and financial analysis, waste
management, waste reduction technology, particularly resource recovery/recycling and
clean technology. Training and equipment for EPCSL will be partly financed under
Package H of the technical assistance schedule.
ACTION PLANS FOR INDUSTRIAL POLLUTION CONTROL
40.   Two major polluters within the Dianchi Basin, the Yunfeng Paper Mill and
Kunming Steel General Company, have identified funding sources apart from the
Bank/IDA loan and have agreed that their pollution abatement action plans be incorporated
within the scope of the project. A third polluter, the Kunming Agricultural Chemical
Factory, also has abatement plans, but has yet to identify funding sources for them. In view
of the possible impact of this plant on the municipal wastewater investments, the municipal
government has undertaken to close or move the plant within three years by not later than
June 30, 1999. Copies of all three plans are available in the project files, while those of
Yunfeng and Kunming Steel are summarized below.
41.   The Yunfeng Paper Mill currently discharges black liquor from the pulp cooking
section, with a chemical oxygen demand (COD) concentration of about 18,000 mg/l to a
storage pond, from which it is flushed during the rainy season. The wastewater from pulp
washing and from the paper coating workshop discharges directly to the Tanglangchuan.
Yunfeng's planned investments would recover much of the alkali from the black liquor,
reducing total pollution loads and easing treatment. An expanded treatment plant would
treat the remaining wash water currently discharging directly to the river. In this fashion,
removal rates of COD, biological oxygen demand (BOD), and suspended solids would all
increase by 80 percent or more compared to the current situation.
42.   The Kunring Steel General Company currently discharges an array of toxic and
other pollutants, including 10 tons of cyanide, over 20 tons of phenol and 350 tons of oil
into the Tanglangchuan. Although this represents a substantial decrease over 20 years ago,
the plant uses (and then discharges as wastewater) almost the entire flow of the
Tanglangchuan during the dry season. To meet water needs for increased production, they
plan to move to very high levels of water recycling, and for that need to reduce pollutant
concentrations in their wastewater. Their strategy is to separate the various water streams,
allowing immediate reuse of cooling water. Other water will pass through one of two new
wastewater treatment plants before reuse. The company estimates that overall recycling
rates can climb from the current 29 percent to over 80 percent.



-163 -                     ANNEX 13
ANNEX 13: MUNICIPAL FINANCES
ASSUMPTIONS FOR FINANCIAL PROJECTIONS
1.    The assumptions made when calculating the projections of the on-budget revenue
and expenditure statements of (a) Yunnan Province; (b) Honghe and Qujing Prefectures;
(c) Gejiu, Kunming and Qujing Municipalities; and (d) Chenggong and Jinnin Counties, for
the period 1995-2002 are set out below. On-budget revenues are those arising from taxes
levied on enterprises operating in the these areas; off-budget revenues consist of special fees
to enterprises and are used chiefly for capital expenditures.
Revenue Forecast
2.    The actual (1992-94) on-budget revenue figures have been derived from data
provided by the provincial, prefectural, municipal and county governments. Between 1992
and 1994, the average annual increase in revenue was between 21 and 68 percent in current
terms, except for Qujing Prefecture, where there was a decrease of 9 percent. In 1994, the
Central Government restructured the tax system, bolstering its share. As a result, there were
marginal declines in revenue in 1994 from 1993, except for Qujing Municipality, Honghe
Prefecture and Chenggong County which posted gains of between 4 and 38 percent. To
estimate future revenues, an overall average annual increase of about 10 percent in real
terms has been assumed using 1994 as the base year.
Expenditure Forecast
3.    Historically, on-budget expenditures have registered between 98 to 100 percent of
on-budget revenues. There was little change in that ratio in 1994 when the tax system was
restructured (para. 2), except in Qujing Municipality and Prefecture, and Chenggong and
Jinnin Counties where expenditures rose marginally as a percent of 1994 revenues. To
estimate future expenditures, the ratio of expenditures to revenues is assumed to be
generally between 75 to 100 percent of revenues.
4.    The following tables summarize projected revenue and expenditure data from 1995
to 2002 for the Municipalities of Gejiu, Kunring and Qujing, the main beneficiaries of
project investments, and Yunnan Province.  Projected project expenditures are also
included.

ANNEX 14: FINANCIAL ANALYSIS ASSUMPTIONS
A. WATER SUPPLY COMPANIES
1.    Assumptions to the projections of the main financial statements of the Kunming
Water Supply Company (KWSC) and the water supply division of Qujing Water Supply
and Drainage Company (QWSDC) for the period 1993-2002 are provided below.
2.    Inflation and Exchange Rates. Yearly general domestic inflation rates used in the
projections are, 10.6 percent in 1996, 8.5 percent in 1997, 7.0 percent in 1998, 6.5 percent
in 1999, and 6.2 percent in percent in 2000-02. Foreign price increases are estimated at
2.4 percent for each of years 1995-2002. The exchange rate used to convert foreign costs to
local currency, where applicable, was Y 8.3 per $1.0. It is assumed that exchange rate
adjustments would, on average, maintain purchasing power parity over the projection
period.
Income Statement
3.    Water Sales. Projections of sales are based on "minimum demand" and maximum
capacity figures. Unaccounted-for-water is estimated at 12 percent of production. It is
assumed that whenever demand exceeds supply, the shortfall would be borne by both the
domestic and nondomestic categories of consumers, proportionally.
4.    Average Tariffs. The average prices in yuan per m in current terms are as follows:
1996     1997      1998      1999     2000      2001      2002
Kunming       0.59     0.59      0.59      0.67     0.72      0.76      0.81
Qujing        0.77     0.93      1.16      1.23      1.31     1.39      1.48
5.    Tariffs. The current domestic, commercial and industrial tariffs in Gejiu are
Y 0.60/m3, Y 0.80 m3 and Y 1.00/m3 respectively. They were introduced in August 1995
replacing the previous tariffs of Y 0.49/m3, Y 0.69/m  and Y 0.89/m3. The domestic,
industrial and commercial, construction, and foreign hotels tariffs in effect in Kunming
since January 1, 1996 are Y 0.60 m/?, Y 0.76/m3, Y 1.10/Im3 and Y 1.50 m3, respectively.
The domestic tariff in Qujing is Y 0.80/m3, rising from Y 0.60/m3 introduced in February
1994. The industrial/commercial, foreign hotels and construction tariffs in Qujing are
Y l.10/m3, Y 1.65/mi3 and Y1.50/m3, respectively.



-166 -                     ANNEX 14
6.    Operating Costs. Salaries and wages are assumed to increase at the rates given in
para. 2 above. Energy prices are assumed to increase in line with general inflation.
Materials and other costs are assumed to increase at the general inflation rate. Composite
depreciation rates of 5.0 percent and 6.7 percent have been applied for Kunming and Qujing
respectively. A Municipal Public Facility Supplementary Tax, Value Added Taxes (VAT),
an urban construction and maintenance surtax and an education surtax totaling 13.3 percent
are charged on gross operating revenue in Kunming. VAT, including an urban construction
and maintenance surtax, and an education surtax, totaling 14.3 percent are charged on gross
operating revenue in Qujing. Income taxes of 33 percent apply in both Kunming and
Qujing.
7.    Interest and Amortization Expenses. The proceeds of the IBRD loan and IDA
credit are assumed to be onlent by the Kunming and Qujing Municipal Governments to
KWSC and QWSDC at the standard rate for LIBOR-based US dollar single currency loans
(5.76 percent per year at the time of appraisal), and 0.75 percent per year commitment
charge, over 15 years, including 5 years of grace. Interest is paid annually. Amortization of
loans would be on the basis of equal payments of principal. KWSC and QWSDC would
bear the foreign exchange risk. The municipalities would provide the local capital
counterpart funds as capital grants.
Balance Sheet
8.   Inventories. Inventories have been projected to take into account general inflation
as well as any expanded levels of operations and construction.
9.    Accounts Receivable. Accounts receivable from water sales are assumed to be
25 days' revenue.
10.   Fixed Assets.  Fixed assets are valued at historic costs. In the projections,
construction work-in progress is assumed to be 100 percent accounted for upon completion.
Source and Application of Funds
11.   Working Capital Needs. Working capital needs represent the excess, or otherwise,
of the current year's current assets (net of cash), less the excess of the previous year's
current assets (net of cash) over current liabilities.
12.   Change in Cash.  Change in cash (which includes short-term  investments)
calculates the balance sheet cash balance for the current year.
B. WASTEWATER COMPANIES
13.   Assumptions to the projections of the main financial statements of Kunming
Drainage Company (KDC) and the wastewater units of Gejiu Water Supply and Drainage
Company (GWSDC) and Qujing Water Supply and Drainage Company (QWSDC) for the
period 1995-2002 are provided below.



-167 -                      ANNEX 14
14.   Inflation and Exchange Rates. Yearly general domestic inflation rates used in the
projections are 10.6 percent in 1996, 8.5 percent in 1997, 7.0 percent in 1998, 6.5 percent in
1999, and 6.2 percent in percent in 2000-02. Foreign price increases are estimated at
2.4 percent for each of years 1995-2002. The exchange rate used to convert foreign costs to
local currency, where applicable, was Y 8.3 per $1.0. It is assumed that exchange rate
adjustments would, on average, maintain purchasing power parity over the projection
period.
Income Statement
15.   Sales. Projections of sewerage quantities are based on "minimum demand" and
maximum capacity figures, and at the equivalent of 80 percent of water bills for Gejiu and
Qujing, and 100 percent for Kunming.
16.   Average Tariffs. The average prices in yuan per m in current terms are as follows:
1996     1997      1998      1999     2000      2001      2002
Gejiu         0.37     0.50      0.85      0.98      1.02      1.08      1.30
Kunming       0.18     0.61      0.73      0.86      0.94      1.03      1.09
Qujing        0.43      0.45     0.48      0.51      0.54      0.66      0.66
17.   Operating Costs. Salaries, wages and staff benefits are assumed to increase at the
rates given in para. 15 above. Energy, materials and other costs are assumed to increase in
line with general inflation also. Composite depreciation rates for Gejiu, Kunming and
Qujing are 3.3 percent, 6.6 percent and 6.7 percent. There is no business or income tax.
18.   Interest and Amortization Expenses. The proceeds of the IBRD loan and IDA
credit are assumed to be onlent to the Kunming Drainage Company and the wastewater
units of GWSDC and QWSDC by their respective Municipal Governments at the standard
rate for LIBOR-based US dollar single currency loans (5.76 percent per year at the time of
appraisal), and 0.75 percent per year commitment charge over 15 years, including 5 years of
grace. Interest is paid annually. Amortization of loans would be on the basis of equal
payments of principal. The wastewater companies would bear the foreign exchange risk.
The municipalities would provide the local capital counterpart funds as capital grants.
Balance Sheet
19.   Inventories. Inventories have been projected to take into account general inflation
as well as any expanded levels of operations and construction.
20.   Accounts Receivable. Accounts receivable are assumed to be about 62 days'
revenue.

Source and Application of Funds
21.   Working Capital Needs. Working capital needs represent the excess, or otherwise,
of the current year's current assets (net of cash), less the excess of the previous year's
current assets (net of cash) over current liabilities.
22.   Change in Cash.  Change in cash (which includes short-term  investments)
calculates the balance sheet cash balance for the current year.

-174 -                      ANNEX 15
ANNEX 15: ECONOMIC, SOCIAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL
CONSIDERATIONS
A. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
1.    The Bank Group highlighted enhancing environmental protection and alleviating
infrastructure bottlenecks as two of the four areas of concentration under the China
Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) presented to the Board in June 1995. The Bank
Group's close collaboration with the Chinese government covers both the regulatory and
service provision  aspects of urban  environmental management.   The China:
Environmental Strategy Paper sector study led to an Environmental Technical Assistance
Project that assists NEPA in its efforts to find more efficient regulatory methods, improve
staff capabilities and bolster environmental monitoring. In addition to national regulatory
assistance, the Bank Group is currently involved in six urban environmental projects in
12 cities, which incorporate policy, financing and organizational strategies for improved
environmental regulation and service delivery at the provincial and municipal levels.
Another four ongoing projects involving 12 cities include related issues such as
municipal water supply and sanitation needs. The initial round of projects focused on
China's largest cities-Shanghai, Beijing and Tianjin-and those located primarily in the
wealthier northeast and coastal provinces. Environmental pressures remain very high in
those areas and cooperation will continue, as with the recently appraised Second
Shanghai Sewerage Project and the Shandong Environment Project now being prepared.
While the recently approved Hubei Urban Environmental Project marks a shift toward
inland and medium-income areas in central China, the Yunnan Environment Project (and
that in Guangxi Province) further test the feasibility of policy and institutional solutions
in poorer provinces.
2.    Bankwide municipal management sector work (Urban Service Delivery: Finding
the Right Incentives) emphasizes the need properly to assign functional responsibilities
and link revenue to expenditures.  In China's case, environmental services are
appropriately provided at the municipal level, where a high percentage of government
revenue is also captured. China: Urban Environmental Service Management concludes
that the major failing in environmental services has been dependence on municipal
budget transfers to fund services, while greater reliance on user charges would be
affordable, induce resource conservation and create a more dependable income stream.
For that reason, increasing tariffs has been a major focal point of the policy dialogue in
this project. This responds, too, to a major finding in the OED review of Bank water and
sanitation projects (Water Supply and Sanitation Projects: The Bank Experience 1967-
1989), wherein physical performance was generally good, but sustaining financial
viability and overall sectoral development was less successful. Adequate sector revenues
have been so crucial to successful operation of these investments that the Bank now sees



-175-                      ANNEX15
the needed tariff structures as the major sign of municipal commitment and therefore asks
for initial adjustments to be made during project processing. The proposed project
follows this model.
3.    This project thus responds to Bank sector work and sector experience and CAS
objectives, first through an improved regulatory environment, reduction of current
pollution loads and protection of critical water resources and second through
commercialization of operating agencies and improved tariff policy and operating
techniques. The Bank's strategy flows from and supports China's Environmental Action
Plan and Agenda 21, which provides the basis for joint Government and Bank action in
the environmental sector, especially in regard to the urban environment. Finally, .the
provincial focus recognizes the effective decentralization of regulatory and investment
authority in China, and draws on local political power and commitment to induce wider
use of the solutions demonstrated under the project.
4.    This project supports provincial efforts to reduce pollution within the province,
especially within densely populated urban areas, through improving environmental
regulation and providing new models for the efficient organization, and financing of
urban environmental services and for industrial pollution control.  Specifically, the
project would support:
(a)   the more aggressive use of user fees to shift the burden of pollution
abatement to the polluter and induce waste minimization, and new
organizational forms for wastewater providers to allow improved
management tools and to help minimize the cost of abatement;
(b)   expansion of sewerage services, to reduce water pollution, within the
boundaries of Gejiu, Kunming and Qujing, and thus protect the urban
environment and the health of their people and those living downstream of
these cities;
(c)   improvements to water supply in the cities of Kunming and Qujing, to
improve the quality of supply and meet the needs of growing urban
populations;
(d)   the first municipal solid waste landfills meeting national standards in the
cities of Kunming and Qujing with consequent protection of local land and
water resources and flaring of landfill methane to reduce greenhouse gas
emissions and an improvement in solid waste collection and transfer;
(e)   abatement of two major industrial point sources of pollution affecting
Lake Dianchi in Kunming and an industrial pollution control revolving
fund to address less costly pollution abatement efforts and provide a model
for other such funds; and



-176 -                     ANNEX 15
(f)   the provincial and municipal EPBs in their efforts to implement more
effective regulatory instruments.
B. ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
5.    Project formulation across three cities and a large number of sectors presented a
major analytical challenge. Ideally, the allocation of investment should be based on
maximizing the net present value while fully incorporating environmental externalities.
This involves estimating the environmental damnage reduction that would result from a
wide range of possible interventions. Yet current methodologies do not allow quick or
inexpensive, but high confidence, estimates of the impact of interventions across such a
broad choice set. Early economic analysis suggested that investment in wastewater
treatment in the Lake Dianchi Basin would yield substantial returns in terms of averting
or postponing costly future investments in water supply. Project formulation therefore
concentrated on identifying projects that would meet this need in a least-cost way,
thereby maximizing their net present value.
6.    Also, during project identification, it became clear that Yunnan and its cities
needed substantial change in policy and institutional structures along the lines described
in the sector work reported in Chapter 1 (China:  Urban Environmental Service
Management). Policy problems included urban environmental services priced too low to
discourage waste generation or provide the financing needed to build, maintain and
operate services such as water supply and wastewater collection and treatment.
Governnent bureaus providing the services lacked the financial tools to develop effective
tariff strategies or the operating autonomy needed to encourage managerial innovation.
Gaps in environmental regulation and enforcement were also apparent. Therefore,
investment choices were made with a view toward maximizing the impact of policy and
institutional innovation within the province.
7.    Specific investment choices within sectors and cities responded to long-run
municipal government strategies to bring ambient environmental quality or service
standards up to national guidelines. In the case of augmented water supply in Kunming,
and the avoided damage to Kunming's water supply, explicit benefit analysis was
possible and undertaken. In Kunming, the fact that all wastewater discharges into Lake
Dianchi, which also serves as a primary water source for the city, permits a benefit
estimate to be made at a reasonably high degree of confidence. In that case, the cost of
doing nothing different from current practice will have an impact on lake water quality
that forces increased treatment costs and an early shift to new, and more expensive water
sources. Those costs serve as a proxy for part of the pollution damage and the proposed
investment to avert them cost far less than the damage. There are also adverse aesthetic,
health and production impacts that are additional to the increased water supply costs but
are very difficult to quantify with a similar degree of accuracy. The benefit estimates in
terms of averted costs are therefore biased downwards from true benefits. For the
wastewater investments in Qujing and Gejiu and the solid waste handling and treatment



-177-                       ANNEX15
investments in Kunming and Qujing, least-cost analysis to meet specified standards was
used. Based on that analysis, city leaders made investment decisions.
City and Sector Choices
8.    In urban environmental service provision, cities were included in the project based
on the size of the population potentially benefiting from improved services and
considering their value as models to other provincial cities. Yunnan has one very large
city, Kunming, with an urban population of some 1.2 million, and 10 smaller cities, the
largest of which is Gejiu with 215,000 people. After reviewing the potential investments
in some five provincial cities, three were selected for further project development.
Kunming was given the top ranking because of its size and the major water quality and
supply problems it faces. The other two cities are Qujing and Gejiu, which are of a size
and economic strength akin to most Yunnan cities and their experience under the project
will therefore be more readily transferred than that of Kunming.  The province
established a leading group headed by a vice-governor with members from the
environmental protection, planning and finance bureaus at the provincial and/or
municipal level, to review the existing situation and guide any decisions needed on scope
or administrative arrangements.
9.    Each of the selected cities had already prepared environmental improvement plans
that outlined priority service needs, both in terms of the type of service and area of the
city needing service. The plans highlighted the relative lack of sewerage and sewage
treatment within urban areas, with household and industrial wastewater discharging to
rivers and lakes rather than to sewer systems and treatment works, and the absence of
sanitary landfill sites for the safe disposal of solid waste and nightsoil. Further work has
confirmed the choice to extend sewerage systems and ensure treatment and to build
sanitary landfills as the next step in environmental protection. Additional water supply
needs had also to be met if growing urban populations were to have access to safe water
and further commercial and industrial investments be supported.
10.   The strategy for industrial pollution control builds on national and provincial
emissions control law and regulations. The change to "total load control" in 1996
represents a positive response by NEPA and the EPBs to the problems that can occur with
a concentration-based emission control system. For enterprises built before polluting
effluents were regulated, cash flow alone rarely meets the financing needs of process
changes or end-of-pipe treatment that will allow compliance with current effluent
standards. Pollution abatement investments must compete in China's plan-driven credit
market with investments typically offering much higher financial returns to sponsors.
Where banks are able to make loans outside of state-dictated credit plans, they seek high
and rapid return opportunities, a feature uncharacteristic of most pollution-abatement
schemes.
11.   Credit market constraints (described more fully in the China Department's 1995
Capital Market Sector Study (No. 14501-CHA) leave unexploited abatement



-178 -                        ANNEX 15
opportunities in economically and financially viable enterprises that currently have a high
pollution impact. Yunnan Province therefore sought to use part of the loan to fund such
investments. The Province agreed with the Bank on a selection process to identify the
highest-impact polluters, a process that has identified two chemical fertilizer plants for
process change investments that will increase profitability as they abate pollution. These
plants both discharge high levels of pollutants, particularly phosphorus, to Lake Dianchi,
and their cleanup is far less costly than providing municipal treatment services removing
the same mass of pollutants. To provide for such opportunities in smaller enterprises, the
province also established a pollution control loan fund, to be initially capitalized at $10
million, for which it requested Bank support. A number of industries have already made
proposals for investment in production improvements and wastewater treatment under
this fund and an appraisal process is being developed for prioritizing these investments
Organizational and Financing Alternatives
12.   Of the proposed investments, the solid waste will be managed by municipal
environmental sanitation sections, the water by existing municipally-owned enterprises,
the wastewater by newly-formed municipally-owned enterprises and the fertilizer plant
pollution abatement by the enterprises themselves.  In each case, the enterprises or
activities were judged unable to compete effectively for private financing at this stage in
their development, though institutional reforms under the project will help them attain
that goal in the future. None has a history of sufficiently profitable operation to attract
private financing.
13.   In the case of the water and fertilizer enterprises, the lack of profits can be
attributed to the fact that they continue to fall under a tightly-controlled output price
regime, while facing a largely deregulated input market. The fertilizer enterprises will
shift part of their production to unregulated markets, thus partially escaping the
regulatory burden. For water, the project commits the municipalities to regulating prices
such as to allow a positive return. The wastewater enterprises were created to provide a
managerial structure facilitating efficient operation, something absent in their previous
government bureau form. For wastewater, the municipalities have committed to a price
structure that results in break-even operations. Only solid waste remains a government
bureau operation. Here, the current collection operation works sufficiently well that
emphasis was placed on providing technical assistance to cost properly the operation and
allow adequate rates for commercial, industrial and other system users. In all cases, a
project goal is to help create improved service delivery at a reduced cost.
Wastewater Collection and Treatment
14.   Kunming.  Kunming discharges all its wastewater either directly or through
sewers to the rivers and canals which flow into Lake Dianchi. These rivers and canals are
therefore grossly polluted.  Domestic and industrial pollution, erosion and land
reclamation have resulted in the lake ecosystem changing to a turbid, eutrophying water
body, the Caohai (inner lake) being more badly affected than the Waihai (outer lake). See



-179 -                      ANNEX 15
map 27911. The water quality in the lake varies by area and season, but has deteriorated
to worse than Class V over much of the year, well below the level at which it should be
used as a drinking water source. The number 3 water treatment works was closed in
October 1994, in response to the quality deterioration. Output from the number 5 works
was reduced in August, 1995 due to these same problems. Nevertheless, the Waihai
supplies up to half Kunming's water in dry years, creating very strong environmental and
health reasons why Kunming's wastewater should be treated.
15.   The Lake Dianchi water quality study identified phosphorus as the limiting
nutrient for the eutrophication of the lake. The economic analysis therefore concentrated
on investments that would control that pollutant or allow adequate treatment of the
polluted water. The economic analysis of wastewater collection and treatment programs
generally presents challenging analytical problems on the benefit side. The range of
impacts, on amenity, health and commodity production uses, can be described but usually
not easily quantified. However, in this project Kunming's Lake Dianchi water use for
municipal supply creates a condition whereby pollution damage can be modeled and
impacts quantified with a reasonable degree of confidence. The analysis is expressed in
terms of the "Do Nothing More Scenario" (DNS) which is what would happen if nothing
more was done over the next 25 years than is currently being done, i.e., no changes to
current policies, plans and institutions and no further interventions to improve water
quality beyond the two municipal treatment plants now being constructed with nonproject
funds. Lake Dianchi is a receiving lake in which pollutants will build up fairly
predictably over time and this will have inevitable consequences for water supply, the
economy and environment and amenity of the area.
16.   If nothing more is done in Kunming than currently planned there are a number of
factors that will result in substantial future investment and costs:
(a)    With rising incomes, per capita water consumption is projected under DNS
to increase by 40 percent from 180 to 250 liters/day by 2020. Industrial
demand will also rise significantly. Agricultural demand will stay fairly
constant as the irrigated area is unlikely to increase.
(b)   Predicted population growth that will increase the urban population to 2.8
million by 2020, because of which annual renewable water resources of
300 m3 per capita will decline to 195 m3 per capita by 2020, leading to a
significant and increasing water deficit whether worked out in water
balance terms or in terms of potential supply and demand flows.
(c)   Storing water from wet years and reuse of water from Lake Dianchi will
remain the main means of balancing demands until new sources of water
are found. The use of Waihai water will increase particularly in drought
years.  Total demand for water from  Lake Dianchi is currently
251 million m3 per year and is forecast to rise to 332 million m3 in 2000.
3
It has the potential to supply up to 342 million m . However, the pressures



-180 -                       ANNEX 15
on the use of Lake Dianchi water will hasten the date at which lake water
will become unusable for domestic water purposes.
(d)   The switching dates among water supply sources are further affected by
the considerable intra- and interannual variations in precipitation and
runoff and by the need to balance water supply requirements against those
of flood control.  At present the majority of the storage capacity
(100 million mi3) of Songhuaba (which has good quality water) is for flood
control purposes which forces the use of increasingly poor quality Lake
Dianchi water for municipal water supplies.
(e)    Effluent entering the lake will rise substantially. At present, more than 80
percent of Kunming's effluent enters the lake untreated. Other sources of
pollution are lakeside industry (particularly fertilizer, pulp and paper,
pesticide factories and metal smelters), lakeside townships, and runoff,
particularly from mining and fertilizers. It is estimated that currently
781 tons/year of total phosphorus (TP) enter the lake and, if nothing more
is done than currently planned, this will increase to 1,146 tons/year by
2010 (see Table 1). The sources of these loads are known in some detail
and can be forecast under the DNS.

(f)   the significant deterioration in quality of water of the Waihai over the last
10 to 15 years will continue at an accelerating rate. TP concentrations
(0.14 mg/l in 1994) have been forecast by the computer model of the
Waihai to increase to 0.30 mg/l in 2000, 0.40 mg/l in 2010 and nearly
0.50 mg/l by 2020 (see Annex 7, Figure 1). There are also significant
forecast increases in nitrate, dissolved organics and toxic heavy metals, but



-181-                       ANNEX 15
phosphorus concentrations are considered to be the most significant
limiting factor for water supply because of their effect on the levels of
algae in the lake.
(g)   the Waihai is already experiencing difficulties with water treatment and
the costs of treatment will further increase. All water treatment plants
will have to install dissolved air flotation (DAF) process to remove
algae (Water Treatment Plant No 5 already has to use this process).
The practice of adding chlorine to the raw water at inlet will increase
despite the dangers of tri-halo methane (THMs).  Later, more
sophisticated filtration and purification techniques will be needed, at
much higher cost. The costs of this increased treatment are summarized
in Table 2 and details can be found on the project files.
(h)   Later on in the period, the buildup of phosphorus in the Waihai will be so
great that the water will not be suitable for industrial uses forcing
industries to install water treatment plants. Some industries are already
treating industrial water. It is not anticipated that the deterioration of the
Waihai will have any adverse effects on agriculture. It may indeed benefit
from the increased supplies and increased nutrients.
(i)    The DNS will result in all the available water in Songhuaba being used for
domestic purposes and agricultural water being pumped up from Lake
Dianchi. This process has already started and is bound to continue if there
is no effort to save the lake.

Gj)    There is a well-defined limit to the Songhuaba catchment (around 133
