(k)   From the present day to the date of importing water from another
catchment, there is a growing water deficit in the Dianchi catchment that
will inevitably result in reducing the amount of water for agriculture.
Under the DNS this will happen even if Songhuaba agricultural supplies
are completely replaced by pumping from Dianchi. The economic cost is
set out in Table 2. The result in Table 2 is most sensitive to the timing of
water augmentation from outside the catchment. If quality deterioration
slowed more than expected and that need were delayed, say, five years, the
NPV of that damage component would fall to Y 1,263 million and total
averted damage would fall to Y 2,066 million.
(I)    Water demand and supply and increasing pollution levels will dictate that,
from around 2005, Kunming will have to move to sources outside the
Dianchi and Songhuaba catchment for its main source of supply. The next
least-cost source that will satisfy DNS demands until the end of the
scenario period will involve a dramatic increase in both capital and current
costs. The source has been identified by Yunnan Water Conservancy
Bureau as the Yulong Reservoir at Zhangjiu River around 150 km from
Kunming. The conveyance (through open ditch and tunnel) and pumping
of this water and its treatment has been costed by project engineers at June
1996 prices and is entered in the DNS (Table 2).
17.   The result of doing nothing will be a very steep increase in the economic costs of
water, which will adversely affect the welfare of everyone in Kunming. Many living in
rural areas will face a continued shortage of irrigation water. Doing nothing will also
impose significant increased costs on some industries resulting in a loss of
competitiveness. The economic analysis has concentrated solely on additional water
supply costs because of their scale and ease of identification. The analysis has not
attempted to calculate other damages such as that to fisheries in the lake, health and
visual amenity or damage attributable to pollutants other than those that lead to the build
up of algae in Lake Dianchi. The wastewater project will also significantly reduce
suspended solids, heavy metals and biological oxygen demand (BOD) but it is not
possible to assess with sufficient accuracy the additional environmental damage reduction
that can be attributable to this. The damage estimate may therefore be considered as a
conservative estimate of the total amount of environmental damage that would result
from Do Nothing.
18.   The long-run objective of the Kunming wastewater project is therefore to ensure
the reduction or treatment of enough wastewater being generated in the urban area (both



-183-                       ANNEX15
industrial and nonindustrial) to maximize the net present value (NPV) of the investment.
This will be achieved through four integrated strategies:
(a)   constructing trunk sewers;
(b)   reticulating currently unscrewed areas;
(c)   constructing sewage treatment works (STWs);
(d)   maximizing connections to the system
19.   The decision concerning which basic wastewater treatment technologies to adopt
for sewage treatment, e.g., oxidation ponds or a variety of conventional, treatment
systems, was made in favor of the latter on the basis of the excessive land requirements
and costs associated with ponds designed for the climatic conditions in Yunnan. Two key
factors compared were the number and location of STWs and the detailed selection of
processes. The location of the STWs had already been approximately fixed by the
existing Sewerage Master Plan. However, other potential locations were also identified
and analyzed. The network layout has been determined by the topography, trunk sewer
minimization, location of STWs, and existing and proposed urban development.
20.   Thus, comparison of network layouts emphasized the utilization of existing
sewers and ensuring use of the most economical pipe size. Within the city center, the key
issue lay not so much with the network arrangement, but whether to convert the existing
combined sewers into a separate system. The latter will ultimately reduce treatment costs
as STW capacities can be limited as they will only treat sewage flows and not stormwater
flows as they do at present. The construction of a separate sewer system also has
advantages in that it reduces sewer size and should minimize pumping, especially in a
region with large seasonal rainfall variations. Nightsoil is still being collected and
disposed of to land, but this is gradually being phased out as the city develops.
21.   In STW design, the key selection factor was to achieve the most cost-effective
method for phosphorus removal, the pollutant of prime concern in the Dianchi Basin.
Secondary factors include nitrogen, BOD/COD and suspended solids removal. The
processes adopted are those believed to be the most efficient in achieving the phosphorus
objective without compromising the secondary objectives. In addition, the selected
processes have the advantage of being technologies already operating in China.
Dewatered sludge will be disposed of to one of the landfill sites being developed under
YEP. The alternative of agricultural use of sludge has not been pursued due to the lack of
a market for currently produced sludge and potential difficulties with contamination, as
shown in the existing STW. As the treatment options involved the treatment of different
quantities, the optimal solution required the valuation of benefits rather than least-cost
methods. The rate of return based on the implied benefits of removing phosphorus
derived from the "Do Nothing More Scenario" (DNS) for the selected option-a three-
stage, twin-return biological treatment strategy, was 51 percent higher than the three-
stage, single-return biological treatment alternative and 18 percent higher than the five-



-184 -                      ANNEX 15
stage biological treatment option. Chemically-assisted primary treatment alternatives
were considered during project development. However, they did not reduce pollution
sufficiently to maintain water quality below the danger zone for treatment.
22.   In summary, the Kunming wastewater project consists of:
(a)   two new STWs and related interceptor sewer system to serve rapidly
expanding urban areas;
(b)   upgrade and extension of an existing STW serving the city center;
(c)   interceptor sewer networks for the western area of Kunring;
(d)   city center sewer rehabilitation;
(e)   house connections;
(f)   discharge of treated effluent to rivers, canals and Lake Dianchi and sludge
to the new sanitary landfill sites.
This project will complete the construction of STWs in Kunming by providing all
drainage areas of the city with an STW.
23.   The present value over 25 years at a 12 percent discount rate of the Kunming
Wastewater Component at June 1996 prices including O&M costs and M&E replacement
is Y 1,163 million. It is currently forecast that YEP wastewater will maintain phosphorus
concentrations at present levels and within the limits where advanced treatment would not
be required.  However, the 95 percent confidence interval around the phosphorus
concentration estimate does cross into the danger zone for water treatment. Therefore, a
new treatment plant for the Lake is planned using advanced water treatment processes.
The treatment of Lake Dianchi water after YEP may have to use advanced processes
involving ozone and activated carbon. The cost of this has been estimated as Y 556
million in present value terms. When this cost is added to that of YEP wastewater, the
overall cost of the YEP strategy reaches Y 1,719 million. This compares very favorably
with the costs averted of Y 2,761 million (and with the low damage scenario of Y 2,066
million) and provides sound economic justification for the component.
24.   Qujing Wastewater. Qujing discharges its wastewater to the Nanpanjiang River
(headwater of the Pearl River), which also supplies industrial and agricultural water to
Qujing. Historically, the water was of sufficient quality to permit its use as a source of
potable water, but continued failure to capture and treat the city's wastewater has resulted
in serious downstream deterioration. The increasing pollution has reduced the water
quality to worse than Class V, which now limits its use even to industry and agriculture.
To achieve the stated water quality objective of Class IV for the Nanpanjiang River, full
sewage treatment would be required, so a least-cost analysis of treatment options was
carried out. Four main treatment options were considered:



-185 -                      ANNEX 15
(a)   oxidation ponds,
(b)   primary settlement followed by activated sludge,
(c)   extended aeration activated sludge, and
(d)   two-stage activated sludge.
25.   Despite the low operating costs, the large land requirements of oxidation ponds,
together with costs and environmental impact of using available land, led to this option
being rejected. Of the other three variations on the activated sludge process, the least-
cost option was identified as the extended aeration activated sludge at a cost (including
the sewerage system) of some Y 185 million. An evaluation was also made of an
alternative site for the STW, 5 km farther away from the city. This would have incurred
additional cost for the longer inlet sewer and for upgrading the access road (a total
additional cost of some Y 23 million on an overall sewerage cost of Y 38 million)
without any significant benefits except a lower land cost for the STW site. It was agreed
that the Qujing Master Plan should be revised to avoid further development immediately
adjacent to the selected STW site.
26.   The option of providing two STWs, the second to serve the new Western
Development Area after about the year 2010, was also considered. The additional costs
of the sewerage and pumping required to carry the flows to one site were shown to be
outweighed by the economies of scale from a single STW. The reuse of the effluent for
agriculture also favored the location of a single STW in the agricultural area. However,
the authorities judged the overall cost of the project to be still too high. To reduce the
cost of this phase, the decision was taken to provide as extensive a sewerage system as
possible at the outset to intercept all the sewage flows reaching the rivers running through
the city but initially to lower the discharge standard (with only removal of gross solids
and some BOD) in the first phase. This could be achieved by providing a reduced level
of treatment. Although this treatment will not meet the long-run requirements, it will
nevertheless result in significant environmental quality improvements. Two options to
achieve this were identified:
(a)   the first stage of the two-stage activated sludge plant, or
(b)   enhanced primary sedimentation using chemicals.
27.   The activated sludge plant was identified as the least-cost investment. An initial
concern that it would produce an unstable sludge has been responded to by adding a
sludge conditioning tank. A preliminary analysis of enhanced primary treatment was also
made and a detailed costing prepared for two chemicals-lime and ferric chloride. Total
costs were higher than those from the activated sludge plant and this option would cost
more to move to secondary treatment. Land is being set aside for the addition of
secondary biological treatment as soon as that is affordable.



-186 -                       ANNEX 15
28.   In summary, the Qujing project selected as the least-cost option for the project is:
(a)   an interceptor sewer system serving the north and east of the city in this
phase, but designed for the addition (after 2008) of an interceptor
specifically designed to serve the anticipated increased development in the
west;
(b)   a single STW  serving the city, this initial phase of treatment being an
intermediate activated sludge process, with disposal of the resulting stable
sludge to the new landfill site;
29.   Gejiu Wastewater. Gejiu discharges its domestic sewage to Lake Gejiu, which
receives wastewater from tin mining and processing and other industrial activities and is
also used as a balancing reservoir for industrial water supply. As a result, the water
quality of the Lake has deteriorated to worse than Class V. Excess water from the lake
(particularly in times of wet weather) is pumped out of the closed valley in which the city
is located into the Zhadian River to the north. Flooding of Gejiu City occurs when the
capacity of the existing pumping station is exceeded. To achieve the stated Class IV
water quality objective, the sewage and industrial wastewater must be prevented from
entering the lake. The considerable benefits to both lake and downstream river of
completely separating these from the flood water were identified during project
development. Principal options considered were based on the location of the STW, since
there are effectively no significant alternatives to the two interceptor sewers which will
pick up sewage flows around Lake Gejiu.
30.   Four possible STW sites were considered, two by the lake and two beside the road
north out of the city. The lakeside sites were rejected on the grounds that the high value
of land within the city would not make these the least-cost solutions. (Environmental
impact on the surrounding urban areas is a further reason for rejecting these, as was the
higher cost of treatment required to achieve the higher effluent standard for discharge to
the lake.) Of the two sites north of the city, Site 1 was preferred over Site 2 because of its
closer proximity to the city, requiring a shorter inlet sewer. The additional cost of the
inlet sewer to Site 2 would be Y 2 million for the extra 1 km required. The capital cost of
the sewerage and pumping stations for Gejiu is Y 40 million.
31.   The arguments on the treatment process to be adopted are the similar to those for
Qujing, with full treatment options being initially considered. As with Qujing, the
secondary treatment alternative was deemed unaffordable by the authorities. The desired
reduction in project cost was achieved by reducing the level of treatment provided in the
first phase while maintaining the extent of the sewerage system (thus ensuring that all
wastewater currently reaching Lake Gejiu will be collected and treated). An intermediate
sludge system with additional sludge stabilization was also chosen for the first phase of
treatment at Gejiu. Adjacent land has been reserved for the addition of secondary
biological treatment as soon as that is feasible. Despite the lower treatment level, Lake
Gejiu water quality will be greatly improved by discharging the treated effluent to the



-187-                       ANNEX15
Zhadian River and replacing the lost water in the dry season (if required) with high-
quality surface water from controlled discharges from local reservoirs. The risk of
flooding in the city will be greatly reduced.
32.   In summary, the Gejiu project selected as the least-cost option for the project is:
(a)   interceptor sewers around Lake Gejiu, with a sewage pumping station
lifting the flows to an STW on the north side of the city;
(b)   a parallel pumping system lifting floodwater from Lake Gejiu to the
Zhadian River;
(c)   a single STW  serving the city, the first phase of treatment being an
intermediate stage activated sludge system.
Water Supply
33.   Kunming. The Kunming Water Supply component was evaluated on a least-cost
basis to supply estimated demand and by the company average incremental cost of the
new supply with willingness to pay revealed through surveys and the recent price:
(a)   the extension of two existing Water Treatment Plants (WPT);
(b)   construction of a new WTP;
(c)   the extension of existing WTP No. I plus construction of a new WTP.
34.   Option 3 was both least-cost grounds and most robust to the most likely
alternative demand scenarios. All long run studies include the eventual importation of
water, the key being that the combination of effective water demand management and the
proposed projects, together with other interventions, should defer importation as long as
possible. Provided all potential water resources within the catchment are developed, the
indications are that there could be sufficient resources to meet the water demand up to the
year 2020 at 95 percent reliability. This involves continuing use of Lake Dianchi for
municipal water supply, albeit using advanced treatment processes, and therefore justifies
the investment to limit damage to the lake. It also clearly stresses the importance of
protecting the lake as a local source for future use for drinking water supply and in so
doing defer more costly interventions as long as possible.
35.   The raw water source of Songhuaba was selected because it is at present the least-
cost source and although the primary role of Songhuaba is for irrigation and flood
control, small increases in municipal supplies can be obtained at the lowest average
incremental cost. Yield modeling showed that with conjunctive use of Dianchi Lake
water, Kunming demands could be met from this source without further augmentation.
36.   Incremental water supplied under the project will be 65 million m3/year at full
development. It is estimated that some 500,000 new individual connections will be



-188 -                      ANNEX 15
provided mainly in the fast growing suburban areas of north, east and west of central
Kunming. The economic cost of this incremental supply has been estimated to be
Y 0.60/m3
37.   Two small willingness-to-pay surveys have been undertaken which indicated that
a substantial number of people would be prepared to pay that much or more for water.
The more recent study indicated that 72 percent of the respondents already served by
piped water would be willing to pay at least Y 0.60/m3. This study confirmed the results
of a 1993 survey in which 89 percent of the households were willing to pay at least
Y 0.72/m3 for current supply. The difference between these studies likely reflects their
methodological differences or small sample error rather than a shift in willingness to pay.
Kunming increased water tariffs to an average of slightly more than Y 0.60/mi3 in January
1996. The water company has seen no consumption decline, although this is the winter,
low demand, season and not until summer will it be possible to fully assess price
elasticity. Nonetheless, experience at current tariff levels, the willingness to pay surveys
and the new connection program all strongly support the conclusion that the net present
value of the investment is positive. Better estimates of the net present value would
require information about the demand cure when prices are above current levels, but that
information is lacking.
38.   Qujing. The Qujing water supply component was evaluated on a least-cost basis
to supply estimated demand. In Qujing's case, local groundwater and surface water
supplies have been fully exploited by the existing rural and urban users, so new supply
must come from either better use of the current system or new sources at a considerable
distance from the city. In this case, both options would be exploited.
39.   The current supply relies on three small reservoirs feeding three water treatment
plants. The treatment plant capacity totals 81,000 m3/d, but the distribution system
capacity reaches only 55,000 m3/d. Some 26,000 m3/d of potential supply therefore goes
unexploited in normal-weather years (dry-year supply of the reservoirs does not reach
treatment plant capacity). Distribution system investments can augment supply by the
26,000 m 3/d (or 47 percent), at a net present value of Y 0.44/m3 . This contrasts very
favorably with the Y 0.93/m3 1996 tariff. Using a conservative assumption that new
supply becomes available over 1999 to 2003; that only 50 percent of newly available
supply is demanded its first two years, rising to 70 percent over the remaining life of the
project; and using the 1996 tariff of Y 0.93/m3, rather than the higher real tariffs proposed
for later years, the distribution system investment realizes a 15 percent rate of return, or a
Y 4.42 million net present value.
40.   The distribution system expansion would primarily serve areas designed for new
industry and residences, areas now provided with all other infrastructure but the water.
Although Qujing's industrial and population growth has been rapid and is now
constrained primarily by water supply, the investment clearly will be most sensitive to the
offtake of treated water and to the availability of raw water (given drought year supply
problems). Sensitivity tests therefore concentrated on this issue, and the investment was



-189 -                       ANNEX 15
found to be quite robust. For example, even with only 35 percent capacity use in the first
five years of operation, rising to only 50 percent thereafter, the rate of return remains at 7
percent. Use would have to fall to only 30 percent of the new capacity over the life of the
project to drive the rate of return down to zero.
Solid Waste-Kunming and Qujing
41.   Initial choices for the solid waste management projects in both Kunming and
Qujing were based on an evaluation aimed at defining the most appropriate technologies.
A least-cost analysis of options was developed for the optimization of the key
components. The initial evaluation considered potential solid waste treatment and
disposal strategies including recycling, composting, incineration and gasification, and
landfilling. Recycling already plays an active role in each of the cities, capturing most of
the 5 percent of the municipal waste stream that can be profitably recycled, mainly glass,
plastics and metals. The industry is highly competitive, with a combination of state-
owned and private firms providing services and little scope for additional municipal
intervention.
42.   Composting, to recycle organic materials, has been tested in both Kunming and
Qujing, but there have been serious operational and environmental problems, costs have
been high and markets have not supported adequate sales.  Both incineration and
gasification have been shown in other cities with similar waste streams to be substantially
more costly than landfill. The current continuing significant use of coal in Kunming
gives the waste a low combustion value, over half the waste stream being inorganic. This
represents a major problem for both composting and incineration/gasification from which
the residues still require landfilling. Landfill was therefore chosen in both cities for the
disposal of municipal solid wastes.
43.   An evaluation of the collection system in Kunming showed the advantage of
developing two landfill sites to serve opposite sides of the city and significantly reduce
the haul distances and environmental impact. The identification and assessment of
sanitary landfill sites took into account factors including haul distance, life expectancy,
cost, access, land use, land acquisition and resettlement, cover material, ecology,
hydrogeology and potential reuse. Five potential sites were identified for Kunming.
44.   Acknowledging the difficulty in a area like Yunnan of finding a landfill site which
can meet all the impermeability requirements naturally, it was accepted that artificial
linings would have to be provided for at least some part of any landfill site chosen for the
two cities. However, the risk of earthquake damage to the liner and the potential for
groundwater contamination is small, there having been only 14 earthquakes in the region
of not more than 7 (on the Richter scale) over the last 500 years. The preferred sanitary
landfill sites for Kunming were identified as Hongshuitang and Baishuitang with a total
cost of Y 45.8 million and a life expectancy of 21 years. In a similar exercise for Qujing,
in which four sites were subject to a similar assessment, a single site, with a total cost of
Y 11.8 million in the first phase and a life expectancy of 19 years and located between the



-190 -                       ANNEX 15
city's two main urban centers (Qujing and Zhanyi), was chosen at Back Valley of Taihe
Mountain.
45.   In both cases, the least-cost site had, as one of the main decision criteria,
minimization of the joint cost of transport and land acquisition. Since land acquisition
cost correlates very strongly with resettlement impact, this criterion also tends to
minimize resettlement. Sanitary landfills must also comply with national standards,
which require leachate and gas collection. No attempt was made, therefore, to compare
the cost of including these with the damage which would ensue from their absence.
Given the long-run risks inherent in unprotected landfills, the need to provide leachate
and gas collection was accepted and additional impact evaluation was not carried out.
46.   Although solid waste collection and transfer vehicles and equipment have a
relatively short life, the transfer stations which form an integral part of the system have
much longer lives, and therefore need to be designed for flexibility to adjust to changing
situations. The analysis of the waste collection system therefore addressed both the cost
of collection and transfer equipment and the optimization of size, location and design of
small, medium and large transfer stations (STSs, MTSs and LTSs) within the city. Costs
of collection and transfer were analyzed and optimized using the UNCHS WAGS
Compactor Program for equipment ranging from tricycles to container trucks.
47.   Optimization of the numbers of collection vehicles showed that for Kunming the
ideal system should be one with a large number of STSs. Waste is carried direct from
them to the landfill sites in 8-ton containers. However, an evaluation of the land
acquisition and environmental impact aspects of the 35 STSs originally proposed
demonstrated the considerable problems of trying to locate a large number of transfer
stations of any size within the city. Particular environmental problems related to noise in
residential areas and to increased traffic movements during the night. On these grounds,
the decision was taken to limit the number of transfer stations in Kunming and thus build
only 15 STSs and to locate four MTSs in the areas of greatest environmental impact.
Sites for a further two MTSs were identified for future expansion. The evaluation
showed that there was no need at all for LTSs, because the time saving achieved would
not justify the additional capital and operating costs of the LTS and related vehicles.
48.   A similar exercise in Qujing showed that the least-cost system would be one with
five STSs in Qujing City and one in Zhanyi. No MTSs are required because of the short
haul distance to the landfill site.
49.   The key area for sensitivity analysis is in the anticipated changes to the nature of
the waste over the lifetime of the two schemes. The feasibility study for solid waste
demonstrated that the density of the waste is expected to fall by 15 to 20 percent over the
next 10 to 15 years as conversion from coal to gas proceeds in the two cities. In
Kunming, the existing STSs already serve residential and commercial areas where gas
conversion has taken place and so little change is expected for them. New STSs and
MTSs should be designed to allow the use of deeper containers in the future to maintain



-191-                        ANNEX 15
an optimum load capacity for the lower density wastes. The density of the waste will
remain quite high for the foreseeable future, so there is no advantage in the use of
compactor trucks for waste collection during the next 10 to 15 years. These are not
therefore being provided. In the MTSs, the waste loaders can be used to achieve a small
increase in compaction (by applying pressure during loading). The slightly increased
need for additional capacity can be met by adjusting shift hours for the vehicles.
50.   Operational flexibility will be built into waste collection management to allow in-
situ decisions on the need to take waste either to local STSs or to go direct to MTSs
depending on traffic levels and thus save on waste handling and costs. The increasing
need to formalize waste recycling processes as the standard of living in the city rises can
be accommodated by the short-run modification of the collection system and vehicles, but
if any changes in waste quantities reaching the landfills are likely, they can be expected to
decrease, simply extending the landfill life.
Industrial Pollution Control
51.   The industrial pollution control component has three subcomponents, two
involving funding for the two fertilizer factories on Lake Dianchi (ranked as the most
environmentally damaging enterprises on the lake) and the third a pollution control fund
targeting small investments and small-scale enterprises. The first two components were
appraised by the Bank/IDA.
52.   The environmental pollution control subloans facility remains to be established.
The goal of the fund is to serve as a model for the operation of the municipal and county-
run pollution control funds capitalized though pollution levy fees. These are revolving
finds, but to date have lacked effective management, targeting and credit policies. The
fund will seek to minimize management costs, while effectively recycling loans. The
fund will bear credit risk because the bank managing it will play a solely agent role.
Loans will be made at a rate of no less than 150 basis points above the standard interest
rate for LIBOR-based US dollar single currency loans, the margin to be determined by
operating costs and credit risk assessment. The Fund will also face a history of pollution
levy fees as grants increasing the credit risk. The fund will respond to these risks in two
ways, first by careful financial analysis and conservative lending, and second by
concentrating on smaller loans with relatively short repayment periods.
53.   The second part of the Industrial Pollution Control strategy involves direct
investment in two chemical fertilizer plants producing a high proportion of phosphorus
load in Lake Dianchi. The fertilizer factories contribute 16 percent of total phosphorus
(TP) discharge to the Lake and 73 percent of industrial phosphorus loads. The two
factories are Kunming Chemical Fertilizer Factory and Kunyang Phosphate Fertilizer
Factory.
54.   Existing wastewater treatment facilities at the factories are inadequate for present
loads and frequent breakdowns further reduce treatment. In a attempt by the Central
Government to hold down agricultural input prices, the fertilizer industry is subject to



-192 -                      ANNEX 15
strict price control which forces many of them to operate at a loss, made up through
government subsidies.  However, subsidies do not cover adequate operation and
maintenance funds which exacerbates operating pressures on the waste treatment systems.
Kunming Chemical Fertilizer Factory
55.   Kunming Chemical Fertilizer Factory is owned by the Heavy Industry and
Chemical Industry Bureau of Kunming Municipality and is located close to the
southeastern shore of Dianchi Lake. Its products are nitrogen and phosphorus based with
potential capacity of 185,000 tons per year which includes ammonia, phosphoric acid,
nitrogen phosphorus potassium (NPK) and ammonium carbonate (NH4HCO3). The
Factory sustains losses of approximately Y 10 million per year on capital of Y 90 million.
Overall, it discharges 4.83 million m3/year containing 35 tons/year of total phosphorus
(TP) and 555 tons/ year of ammonia-nitrogen (NH3-N) to the Waihai of Lake Dianchi.
56.   The Yunnan Chemical Industry Design Institute feasibility study proposed
production changes including a major investment in a new 80,000 tons/year H2SO4 plant,
which will allow continuous operation and reduce pollution problems arising from
current stop-start operations.  Recovery of raw materials from wastewater will be
increased and recycling of water will improve. The majority of the Y 54 million
investment will help fund the new sulfuric acid (H2SO4) Plant. The remaining sum will
be used in the following way:
(a)   modification to NH3-N production processes;
(b)   modification to monoammonium phosphate (MAP) and phosphoric acid
(H3PO4) production processes;
(c)   modification to water recycling system,
(d)   treatment for MAP and H3PO4 wastewaters,
(e)   modification to water supply system, to drainage,
(f)   general drawing and transport,
(g)   treatment of ammonia wastewater,
(h)   further modification to NH3-N production processes, and
(i)   other capital construction.
The proposed improvements will reduce wastewater discharge to Lake Dianchi of TP
to nearly zero and ammonia to 10 tons/year.



-193 -                      ANNEX 15
57.   Currently the output of the plant is sold at an average of over 50 percent less
than the border price of these products. This adversely affects the financial viability of
the firm and the financial viability of the incremental project because without price
control the firm could put up its prices to about this extent (plus or minus any
differential transport costs between the firm, customer and port) and still remain
competitive. However the firm also receives competitive advantage from subsidized
and controlled prices of its inputs. An initial attempt to adjust the rates of return for
both these effects suggests that the rates of return to the project would not be
significantly higher that those above if the company was operating in a regime of
uncontrolled prices. In fact, the project itself will move the company into a less
controlled environment as the new processes and products are less subject to price
control.
58.   The economic rates of return for the proposed investment were calculated on a
base core of 83 percent capacity utilization. To date, plant sales have been construed
by input shortage, rather than lack of output demands. Relief of the input constraint
should allow full production (which, by industry standards in China is 300 days per
year). The sensitivity analysis focused on the capacity utilization issue, on a proxy for
both operational problems, and lack of member demand. It also looked at input price
shifts in sulfur, one of the key inputs The results of the analysis are given below.
Table 3: PROJECTED ECONOMIC RATES OF RETURN TO THE KUNMING
FERTILIZER FACTORY (%)
Utilization rate                Price changes
83%          70%         60%         Sulfur price up 50%
ERR                 56           45           36                 37
Kunyang Phosphate Fertilizer Factory
59.   Kunyang Phosphate Fertilizer Factory is owned by the Provincial Chemical
Industry Department and is located approximately 69 km from Kunming and southwest
of Dianchi Lake. Its major outputs are more than 24 phosphate ore-based products
weighting more than 300,000 tons per year including phosphate and superphosphate
fertilizer, phosphate chemical products and other products. At present, the Factory owns
fixed assets of Y 150 million and employs 2,900 people. It maintains a modest profit of
Y 3 million per year or a return of 2 percent on capital. It currently discharges 4.1 million
m3/year containing 504 tons/year of suspended solids, 56.5 tons/year of total phosphorus
and 22.9 tons/year of fluoride to the Waihai of Lake Dianchi.



-194 -                      ANNEX 15
60.   The Yunnan Chemical Industry Design Institute feasibility study assessed three
engineering options on the basis that they minimized the quantity of effluent discharged.
The options all involved process and product changes. No options were considered for
the water treatment plants. The proposed production changes will shift the composition
of final products away from certain phosphate products (calcium magnesium phosphate)
to high-value food-grade phosphates. Two new water treatment plants are proposed to
increase recycling of water. The major portion of the Y 75 million investment will be
placed in:
(a)   modification of the 40,000 tons/year H2SO4 Plant,
(b)   two-stage precipitation wastewater treatment facility and
(c)   production facilities for food-grade compounds.
61.   Other improvements are:
(a)   modification to the existing production processes,
(b)   expansion/modification to existing treatment facility,
(c)   modification to water recycling system,
(d)   segregation of drainage,
(e)   discharge pipeline,
(f)    water supply station and
(g)   importation of technical know-how.
62.   After the investment in production processes, overall TP discharge will decrease
from 56.5 tons/year to 0.84 tons/year.
63.   Similar to the Kunming Chemical Fertilizer Factory, economic analysis was
performed on Kunyang with and without the proposed investment. Sensitivity analysis
was performed by varying output and observing its effects on the economic internal rates
of return (ERR). The results of the analysis are shown in Table 4.



-195 -                      ANNEX 15
Table 4: PROJECTED ECONOMIC RATES OF RETURN TO THE KUNYANG
PHOSPHATE FACTORY (%)
Utilization rate                Price changes
83%         70%          60%         Sulfur price up 50%
ERR                 33           29           24                 37
64.   Currently the output of the plant is sold at an average of between 30 and 40
percent less than the border price of these products.  This adversely affects the
financial viability of the firm and the financial viability of the incremental project
because without price control the firm could put up its prices to about this extent (plus
or minus any differential transport costs between the firm, customer and port) and still
remain competitive. However the firm also receives competitive advantage from
subsidized and controlled prices of its inputs. An initial attempt to adjust the rates of
return for both these effects suggests that the rates of return to the project would not be
significantly higher that those above if the company was operating in a regime of
uncontrolled prices. In fact, the project itself will move the company into a less
controlled environment as the new processes and products are less subject to price
control.
65.   Overall, the proposed investment in the two plants would upgrade their
production processes, change some of the final outputs and allow profitable and
cleaner operations. Phosphorus discharge will drop from 93 tons per year to below 2
tons. The amount of other pollutants will also drop. As the preferred options undergo
further review, the economic and financial analysis will be amended appropriately.
Sensitivity analysis on the two investment indicated that Kunming Chemical is more
robust than Kunyang Phosphate.
C. AFFORDABILITY
66.   The affordability issue arises only with the water and wastewater components of
the proposed project. The industrial pollution interventions will proceed only if the
enterprises can demonstrate that they will remain financially sound after the investment.
Solid waste charges will rise only for industrial and commercial users of the new
landfills. Affordability of the new water and wastewater systems was tested against
current consumption patterns and average income of the poorest 10 percent of the urban
population in the three cities. The proposed first-year tariff would consume an average of
less than 2 percent of the income of the poorest 10 percent of the population, with
wastewater fees based on 80 percent of water used. Indeed, because the poor are less
likely than other households to have water piped to the, even the 2 percent figure
represents an upper bound.

Long-Term Strategic Risks
71.   Increase Environmental Pressures. Rapid economic and population growth in
the Dianchi Catchment may simply exert too much pressure on available water and land
resources, and the emergent planning and environmental controls may not be able to cope
with this. Furthermore, the degree of ecological integrity and carrying capacity of the
environment are key parameters which will determine the level and intensity of urban and
rural development in the catchment but many of these are unknown at present. Increased
environmental pressures are from the following:
(a)    Policy Conflicts I-Inappropriate Resource Pricing. The underlying
causes of a number of environmental problems in the Dianchi Catchment
are in part a product of policies operating at the national level, especially
as they relate to resource pricing and agricultural support policies, such as
price   controls  on   fertilizer  supply.              The   longer-term    transition   to
sustainable development will require changes at the national level to
resource pricing and agricultural development objectives and plans.
(b)    Policy Conflicts II-Going for Growth. Other areas of potential conflict
include the push for economic growth and need to protect the
environment. In the short run it is tempting to "go for growth" and then



-199 -                     ANNEX 15
return to the task of cleaning up, rehabilitating and protecting the
environment. This strategy is very risky and is unlikely to be successful
given the existing precarious position of water resource shortages and
degradation.  The latter will almost certainly put a brake on future
development, and thus undermine future income and employment growth,
if no pollution control and water conservation measures are implemented
immediately.
(c)   Rapid Growth in Rural Areas. Increases in TVE and other rural-based
activities in the catchment area will increase the demand for scarce water
resources and increase pollution loads. Rural-based activities are the most
difficult to control as they are currently outside the urban master plan
areas. This threat principally relates to Dianchi Lake (control apply in the
Songhuaba Reservoir area).
(d)   Rapid growth of Kunming. Population growth (including the floating
population in the urban and peri-urban areas) will lead to intensified
competition for land and water while at the same time increase pollution
loads. A key risk here is that the pace of urban development exceeds the
financial and institutional capacity of Kunming Municipal Government
(KMG) to implement urban environmental service improvements and
pollution control measures.
72.   Mitigation measures designed to mediate or avoid these risks include
strengthening the development planning system, improving resources pricing, and
improving the provision of environmental services provision. A key focal point of the
YEP is to strengthen the planning and provision of environmental services.
Complementing these initiatives are broader planning proposals in DEAP, which are
aimed at providing effective tools for regulating development in the Lake Dianchi and
Songhuaba Basins. The IPC Components of the YEP and DEAP, coupled with improved
resource pricing and pollution charges, are also designed to support industry and
introduce pollution prevention/pollution control measures. The objectives for managing
development include limiting the scale of growth to the resources available to support it.
Institutional Risks: Failure to Manage Environmental Pressures
73.   Increasing environmental pressures necessitate that institutions must have the
capacity to manage these pressures. Environmental management in the catchment is
marked by weak and fragmented institutions, lack of clear lines of authority and conflict
and lack of cooperation among institutions at all levels. To date there has been a
reluctance to enforce regulations and make polluters pay. There will be an overriding
need for cooperation and coordination among institutions specifically responsible for
environmental protection and development control around Lake Dianchi and the
Songhuaba Reservoir. A critical component of the success of the YEP and DEAP is the



- 200 -                                      ANNEX 15
enforcement by Yunnan Provincial Government (YPG), KMG and the KEPB of the
existing and enhanced environmental protection measures and regulations.
Financial Risks
74.       There  are  a  number  of longer-term   financial risks  to  the  project.   There  is
uncertainty regarding the state of municipal finances. The major concern here is the
extent of off-budget and contingent liabilities incurred by KMG and other municipal
governments and their impact on the affordability of the YEP at the municipal level. It is
important that there is no delay in accessing funds in support of the project and
complementary investments required to support overall environmental improvement in
the Dianchi Lake Basin.
